"Not a bad game country this here, I
fancy, sir," he observed, "though there don't
seem to be much preserving hereabouts. I
hadn't seen no pheasants yet, nor no rabbits
neither. But there's a sight o' ducks in some
o' they petty," pointing to a pool about a
quarter of a mile to their left, and using the
old Wealdshire expression, which made Gerard '
feel almost as if he were at home again. " I
went out shooting' the other morning', and I
killed eight couple o' ducks in no time. I
could ha' sold they ducks for four shillings a

couple in Ashford Market," continued the
old poacher, reflectively ; " but, Lord bless
you," with a half-regretful, half-contemptuous
expression on his face, "there ain't no one
to buy 'em here ; northern but they niggers
and such-like. And then there's them snakes
too ; I precious nigh trod on one just as I
were a-going to shoot into a lot o' ducks ;
and they say that if you get a bite from some
on 'em, why, it's all up with you. Yes, sir,
it's a fine country, no doubt ; but there's some
queer things in it too ; and I aren't sure
as Easthampton ain't the best place after
all. Howsomever, here I be for the presents''


With such improving conversation Diadems
beguiled the time, Tom occasionally joining
in the conversation, and showing himself to
be of a remarkably keen and observant disposition.
From him Gerard gathered that Mr
Montenello had a large concession of land
from the Venezuelan Government, the title
to which had not as yet been disputed by
successive administrations ; and that he was,
moreover, the owner of large herds of cattle.

which wandered freely over the llanos or
savannahs, and gave employment to a considerable
number of stockmen or llaneros,
who, from Tom's account, all looked upon Mr
Montenello as a sort of feudal chief. He
spoke, moreover, of coffee plantations, systematic
fruit cultivation, and other industries
carried on by this Venezuelan Monsieur Mon-tonga,
as Gerard began to think him.
"And a right good master he is, too,"
observed Tom, in conclusion; "why, there
isn't one o' those chaps that he doesn't
know all about, and he takes just as much
interest in 'em as if they were his own sons.
Why, there's a regular village of 'em all
round about our place ; and he's taught 'em
to live respectable, and learned 'em to read
and write, and he's got a priest, and a doctor,
and I don't know what else." And Mr
Lockyer, who had apparently not altogether
abandoned his seafaring habits, rolled his
quid in his cheek, and expectorated with
much emphasis.

They had now been riding for about two
hours, and had traversed some ten miles of

the savannah. So far, the signs of civilised
life, since leaving the road, had been few
and far between ; an occasional herd of cattle
in the distance, attended by one or two
solitary horsemen, being the only indication
that the plain was frequented by human
beings. Now, however, it became evident
that they were approaching inhabited dwellings.
The grassy track became more beaten
and diversified by wheel-marks ; the herds
of cattle became more numerous, and already
the white walls of houses could be seen
dotted about against the dark green of a
wooded eminence about a couple of miles
away. A team of mules met them, the
drivers of which looked inquiringly at Gerard,
and exchanged greetings in Spanish with Tom,
while Diadems waved his hand to them ina
friendly and patronising manner. The heat
of the sun was now getting almost insupportable,
and Gerard was glad to think that his
ride was nearly over. They pushed on at a
rapid trot, and were soon among a collection
of neat, whitewashed cottages and cabins, for
the most part thatched with palm leaves.

and surrounded by patches of cultivated
ground. Passing through these, and by
various fold yards and outbuildings indicative
of a large cattle-farming establishment,
they came upon an open space tastefully
planted with tropical trees and flowering
shrubs ; and wheeling to the left into a sort
of courtyard, pulled up in front of a spacious
and well-built hacienda.

LOS Angels.

''Mr Montenello is away, sir, I think," said
Tom, as they dismounted at a flight of
wooden steps leading up to a door which
opened into a large, cool-looking hall or
vestibule ; " but there'll be some of the people
about who will look after you." And as he
spoke, two or three negro servants emerged
from various corners where they had been
basking in the sun, and commenced chattering
volubly. Attracted, too, by the hubbub,
a " coloured gentleman " of somewhat higher
position appeared at the top of the steps, and
motioning the inferior domestics to get out
of the way, approached Gerard in the most
urbane and condescending manner. The state
of affairs being explained to him by Tom,
he was so obliging as to express his entire
approval, and begged Gerard to come in and

make himself at home. "Mass' Montenello,
sal, he away after them dam Indians, sal ;
dam scoundrels, rogers too ; call 'themselves
rogers, nothing but thieves, I says. Walk in,
sal ; Mass' Montenello berry glad to see you.
Me Pedro, sal. Mass' Montenello steward,
head man, sal."

So Gerard entered, and found himself in
the house of the man of whose existence he
had first heard from the lips of his far-away
love, and about whom there seemed, apart
from his association with her, to be such
a halo of mystery aud interest. Passing
through the hall, which was tastefully
arranged with ferns and tropical plants, and
cooled by a little fountain, into the marble
basin of which a tiny jet of water plashed
and tinkled with a deliciously refreshing
sound, he followed the sable major-domo into
a small apartment that was evidently the
sanctum of the master of the house. The
room was hung round with guns, rifles, and
various sporting implements, and one or two
handsome skins were stretched on the floor.
A large book-case ran across one end of the

room, and a few prints and water - colour
sketches adorned the walls. There was little
actual furniture, however, beyond a large
writing-table and one or two wicker lounging
- chairs ; and had it not been for the
unmistakably tropical appearance of the sun-blinds
and the verandah outside, it might
have passed for the "den" or smoking-room
of an English country squire. But there
was something that particularly attracted
Gerard's eye as he glanced round the room.
It was a portrait of a lady in a stand on
the writing-table. Even at a distance he
thought he recognised the face, and a closer
inspection showed that he was right, and that
it was a beautifully coloured photograph of
Florence Graham.

Knowing the intimacy that existed between
them, Gerard could hardly feel much surprised
at finding a photograph of his ladylove
in the possession of Mr Montenello. He
was, in fact, inclined to feel more annoyed
than surprised, and to indulge in some resentment
against the man to whose abode
accident had so curiously wafted him, for

being the owner of what he would have
so prized himself. " Hang the fellow ! " he
thought, " why the devil should he have
this lovely thing of her, while I haven't
got even the commonest photograph?" A
moment's reflection, however, showed him
that there was probably not much ground
for jealousy on his part, and that nothing
was more natural than that an old friend ina
distant country should be in possession of
such a portrait. In the meantime, it was
very nice to come across a likeness of her
in such a place, and he felt very tender
as he gazed upon the face that was so dear
to him — dearer than ever, he thought,
in spite of all the mystery and uncertainty
that seemed to place such a barrier between
them.

He was roused from his reverie, however,
by the return of his dusky friend, who had
left him alone for a moment, and who was
now followed by two or three other domestics
of the same complexion, grinning from ear
to ear, and anxious to have a peep at the
stranger. These intruders were, however,

sternly repressed by the major-domo, anxious
to exhibit himself in Gerard's eyes as a personage
of the first importance.

Get away, you niggers ! " he exclaimed,
in disgust. " What debbil you want here ?
Gentleman not want to see your ugly face."
And then, addressing himself to Gerard, 'like
some breakfast, sal — luncheon, sal ? Get
something ready directly, sal."

Gerard's long ride had given him a healthy
appetite, and he readily accepted the offer of
refreshments, leaving it to his deputy host
to decide as to the character of the repast.
He had not long to wait ; and in twenty
minutes was sitting down, in a cool little
Indian-matted dining-room, before a first-rate
omelet and some cunning preparation
of fowl, accompanied by a bottle of excellent
light claret, to all of which he did ample
justice. Nor could he complain of suffering
from solitude, for he was waited upon with
much assiduity by his friend Pedro, who lost
no opportunity of improving the visitors
mind by his own edifying conversation. He
was glad, indeed, when his repast was over,

to get rid of his loquacious attendant by
intimating that he was rather tired, and
would prefer being left alone till Mr Montenello's
return, which he was given to understand
would probably be in another hour or
two. So he established himself in a wicker
chair in the verandah, and lighting an excellent
cigar that had been given him by
the Governor of San Fernando, prepared fora
little quiet enjoyment.

Through the wooden blinds of the verandah
he obtained a peep of a pretty lawn, planted
with all manner of flowering shrubs, and
with one or two stately palms towering
above a mass of dark-green foliage ; beyond
stretched an indistinct line of bush and prairie,
shimmering in the golden haze of a tropical
noon ; and in the far distance rose the blue
outline of the mountains. Not a sound was
to be heard but the eternal buzzing of the
countless insects, and an occasional croak
or screech from some domesticated cranes
and other curious birds that wandered about
among the tree-ferns and rhododendrons ina
listless manner, with apparently a vague

idea of picking up something to eat. Even
the chattering of the negroes and miscellaneous
loafers in the courtyard had been
subdued by the intense heat, and all nature
seemed to have acquiesced in the necessity
for a mid-day siesta.

Gerard, reclining in his wicker chair and
deriving much satisfaction from his cigar,
was by no means disinclined to succumb
to the general dolcefar niente. His interest
had at first been aroused by a glimpse of
one or two shapely Creole girls, flitting round
the corner of the verandah, and evidently
by no means devoid of curiosity regarding
himself. But these nymphs had now disappeared,
and he was left entirely to his
own reflections. These, it is hardly necessary
to say, had a tendency to gravitate ina
general way towards Florence. " Fancy
if she could see me here," he thought, " in
this chap's house. She'll probably hear about
it, by the way, as he's pretty certain to
write and tell her. By Jove ! I might get
him to give me a message or something when
I go back, that would be an excuse for seeing

her again." Then he fell back upon the old
speculation as to what the connection between
old Mr Baldwin and Mr Montenello
could be, and who Florence was herself. As
to this, however, he was determined to try
and find out something from his present host.
I'll just ask him straight out," he thought,
and if he seems a decent sort of fellow,
why, hang it ! I'll tell him all about it. He
can't possibly be spooning her himself, and
it's quite likely he might do me a good
turn." But the more he pondered the
sleepier he became, until, by degrees, his
head drooped upon his breast, the half-finished
cigar escaped from his lips, and he
sank into a profound slumber.

He must have slept for more than an
hour, when he was aroused by the trampling
of horses outside, accompanied by a
confused murmuring of voices, among which
he recognised the consequential accents of his
friend Pedro ; and in another moment the
door was thrown open by that gentleman,
and a tall figure, attired in riding-boots and
a loose tunic belted round the waist, entered

the room. Gerard jumped up in a hurry, and
advanced to meet him. It was just as he had
expected. As he told a certain person not very
long afterwards, he could not feel actually
surprised, but he was unable to repress a
start, and a strange, indescribable feeling for
which he was unable to account, as, in spite
of the change of dress, he recognised in a
moment the man whom he had met by the
covert side that bright October afternoon at
Easthampton.

For a moment, however, the recognition
was not mutual. The room was darkened
by sun-blinds, and the master of the house
had evidently not altogether grasped the
circumstances under which Gerard had made
his appearance, and looked upon him as a
chance traveller. But the reception of his
guest was none the less courteous on this
account. " Very glad to see you, sir," he
began. " I am sorry I was not in when
you arrived, but I have been out on some
troublesome business since daylight. I
hope my people have looked after you,
however ? "

VOL. III. D

Gerard hastened to assure him that he
had been well taken care of, and then to
explain his uninvited presence. "I feel as
if I ought to apologise for paying you
such an unceremonious visit, without having
the pleasure of your acquaintance," he
began; "but Sir Henry Turner, with whom
I have been staying, assured me that I
might venture to do so, otherwise I
should really have felt quite ashamed of
myself. I had better give you his letter
at once."

Pray don't talk of it, sir," interrupted
his host. " I should be delighted to see
you anyhow, but all the more so for
being a friend of Sir Henry Turner's. We
don't often see an Englishman about here,
I can tell you, and a visitor is a great
excitement to us. By the way," breaking
open the seal of the letter, " I ought
to have inquired your name."

*' My name is Cour " replied Gerard ;

but the words were hardly out of his
mouth when Mr Montenello, who had
scanned the first few lines of the letter.

gave a perceptible start, and shot a glance
of keen inquiry at him that for the
moment was most embarrassing. He recovered
himself, however, in an instant,
and remarked, " Ah yes, I see. Well,
Mr Courtenay, I shall be delighted to do
anything I can to show you what there
is to be seen here, though I'm sorry to see
by Sir Henry's letter that you will only
be able to stop a day or two. However,
we'll do the best we can. This is our
rainy season, and we are very lucky in
having these few fine days. Now then,
if you'll come with me, I'll see where we
can put you up. You mustn't expect anything
very grand here, but I daresay
you won't mind roughing it a little."

Gerard followed in silence. It had been
impossible for him to avoid being struck
by the remarkable effect that his name
appeared to have produced upon his host.
What the deuce can it mean ? " he thought
to himself. " By Jove ! she's told him, that's
what it is, and naturally he'd be a bit surprised

at seeing me here ! " And his spirits
rose as he reflected that Florence would hardly
have said anything about it unless she had
been a good deal interested in him either
one way or the other, or, at any rate, would
not have said enough to account for Mr
Montenello's singular demeanour. " My darling,"
he thought to himself; "fancy if she
really cares for me, after all ! " And he felt
more and more determined to take Mr Monte-mello
into his confidence, and endeavour to
enlist his sympathies.

Mr Montenello led the way across the
hall down a passage. " I think this will
do for you," he said, pausing at a door
about half-way down. Gerard followed him
into the room, a neat though scantily furnished
apartment, with a camp bedstead and
fittings to correspond. The room was somewhat
dark, and his host went to the window
and threw open the jalousies. As he glanced
round the room, Gerard noticed that his eye
seemed to light upon a water-colour sketch
hanging over the bed. "No, by the way,"

he continued, rather hurriedly, " I think
there's another room that you will like
better," and he hastily shut the blinds and
went out of the room. Gerard glanced at
the picture as he passed, but was unable to
make out anything in the uncertain light
beyond a very ordinary English landscape
a large house, standing apparently in a
park, with a background that might be hills,
or woods, or anything. But somehow or
other it seemed to remind him of a place
that was not unfamiliar to him ; and .the
incident only served to increase his perplexity,
and to make him resolve more firmly
than ever to endeavour by some means or
other to get to the bottom of the mystery
that unquestionably surrounded his host.

There was evidently, however, plenty of
accommodation in the hacienda; and Gerard
was soon established in a room with the
arrangements of which the master of the
house appeared perfectly satisfied. Here
they were joined by Pedro with Gerard's
things; and Mr Montenello having excused

himself on the plea of business, Gerard was
once more left to his own devices. " It's
too hot just now to do anything much out
of doors," observed his host, " but it will be
cooler in another hour or so, and then, if
you like, we will take a stroll round before
dinner. I daresay you have had enough
riding already for to-day."

Having unpacked and arranged his things,
it appeared to Gerard that the best thing
he could do would be to go back and sit
in the verandah until his host should rejoin
him. So he retraced his steps, first to the
little dining-room, and then to the room into
which he had first been shown, with a general
idea of having another look at Florence's
photograph. As he crossed the hall, he
observed two or three doors, through one of
which, being half open, he took the liberty
of peeping. He found himself in a large,
well - proportioned apartment, which might
have made a handsome drawing - room, but
which was totally destitute of furniture, and
evidently never made use of. His curiosity

being aroused, and there appearing to be
no one about, he opened another door, and
found another smaller room in the same
condition. It seemed evident, in fact, that
the only inhabited reception-rooms were the
two with which he had already made acquaintance.
'well, it's quite clear the old
boy's a bachelor," thought Gerard ; " there
are evidently no ladies about. Widower,
though, more likely; a fine old fellow like
that must probably have been married sometime
or other; however, that's no affair of
mine."

He then sauntered into the little study
or sanctum, and took another good look at
Florence's photograph. Having derived much
satisfaction from this, it suddenly occurred
to him to go back and look at the picture
in the first bedroom into which Mr Montenello
had taken him, the sight of which
appeared to have changed that gentleman's
views as regards his guest's accommodation.
He could not mistake the room ; it was the
first door on the right down the passage.

He turned the handle noiselessly, and entered
with the feeling of being about to commit a
petty larceny. Creeping softly to the window,
he let in a ray of light, and then proceeded to
examine the mysterious work of art. There
was certainly nothing remarkable about it ; a
somewhat hard water-colour sketch, evidently
the work of an amateur, whose instincts
tended more towards a map-like and Chinese
representation of the original than towards
any artistic ideal. But it was evidently
correct, as regards the windows and doors
of the house, at least, to a nicety; and
Gerard recognised it in a moment. There
was the conservatory, with its curious flight
of outside steps leading up to the drawing-room
; the great staircase window, and a
very peculiar and obtrusive buttress at the
corner by the north porch, of which excrescence
the artist had conscientiously made
the most. There was no room whatever for
doubt ; it was a sketch of Easthampton
Park.

Gerard was more puzzled than ever. There

seemed to be no end to the mystery. Here
was a man, who was intimately associated
with Florence Graham, who had, as it were,
abducted Diadems from Easthampton, and
who, it was clear from his having that sketch
in his possession, was in some way or other
interested in the place itself. What the devil
did it all mean ? And why should there have
been any objection, as certainly had appeared
to be the case, to his seeing the sketch?
However, that didn't matter so much ; but
it was absolutely necessary that he should find
out about Florence. And he returned to his
seat in the verandah, feeling almost as if he
had a personal grievance against Mr Montenello,
and resolved to get to the bottom of
the mystery by some means or other before
he went away.

An hour or two later, Gerard was sitting
at dinner with his host, having in the meantime
been taken on a short tour of inspection
in the immediate vicinity of the hacienda.
He had been much struck by the regularity
and order displayed on every side, and by the

evident care and labour that had been bestowed
upon the creation of a sort of model
farm or settlement in this lonely South
American prairie. Enough but neat and
comfortable stabling for apparently innumerable
horses ; cattle-sheds and pens without
end ; carpenters' and blacksmiths' shops ;
long rows of neat labourers' cabins, bordered
by trim gardens ablaze with tropical flowers ;
and, in short, every apparent requisite for
the maintenance of a small social community,
not omitting a neat little red-roofed chapel,
with a schoolhouse adjoining, the necessity
for which latter was demonstrated by the
numbers of woolly-headed children of various
sizes and shades of colour rolling and playing
about in front of the cabin doors.

As they sat alone after dinner, Gerard was
unable to avoid complimenting his entertainer
upon the completeness of everything about
the place, and the order and discipline that
appeared to reign everywhere. " You seem
to have established a regular little kingdom
here," he said, " and have certainly got all

your subjects in wonderful order. I should
never have dreamed of finding such a place
in Venezuela. By the way, though," he
continued, endeavouring to appear at his
ease, but conscious of failing signally in the
attempt, " I think I've heard about you in
England. I — I think you know a Miss
Graham, who lives near Shawfield, in Weald-shire,
don't you ? I remember hearing her
mention your name."

Mr Montenello looked at him hard, but
manifested no surprise. " Yes," he replied,
slowly, and as if weighing his words, *^ yes,
I know Miss Florence Graham. It was at
Netherdean that you met her, I suppose ? "
he added, rather abruptly, and, as appeared
to Gerard, with a certain sternness in his
manner.

"No, it was at a ball at Shawfield,"
replied Gerard, his thoughts reverting to
that memorable evening ; " that's to say,
it was there that I was introduced to her.
I had seen her before, but I didn't know then
who she was."

"Ah," returned his host, dryly. "Well,
I daresay you didn't expect then that you
were ever going to meet a friend of hers in
Venezuela. Yes," he continued, as if anxious
to change the subject, "I think the place
here is in pretty good order, and I've
managed to make some of these fellows
see that a little regular work is not a
bad thing ; and I believe they trust in
me to a certain extent. But I have been
the best part of thirty years at it, soone
ought to have something to show for
that."

Thirty years ! " echoed Gerard ; " you
must be very fond of this country, I suppose
? "

"Yes," replied Mr Montenello, with a
certain sadness in his voice, and a strange,
weary look in his face, that for the moment
made him look very much older, " it has
been my lot in life, and perhaps it has
all been for the best. Anyhow, I hope I
have done my duty, and that when I am
gone my name will perhaps be not altogether

forgotten here. But come," he added, "it's
no use talking about that. We will have
a cigar, and I daresay you will not be sorry
to turn in early. Pedro, bring the coffee
into the verandah."

THE BEGINNING OF THE END.

Gerard was roused from a sound, dreamless
sleep by the tramping of footsteps and a
murmur of conversation, not only in the
passage outside his door, but, as it seemed
to him, in the court-yard under his window.
He started up, and, rubbing his eyes, stumbled
out of bed, with a vague impression that
something was going on. It was early dawn.
Far away into the obscure distance stretched
the long line of the apparently boundless
llanos, like a calm tropical sea, a dark and
mysterious expanse merging gradually into
a misty horizon, where a few faint streaks
betokened the advent of another day. A
solemn stillness pervaded the whole face of
nature — that strange, unearthly quiet which
immediately precedes sunrise. Immediately
outside the hacienda, however, all was bustle

and confusion. Lights gleamed here and
there ; horses were being led about ; there
was much jabbering in Spanish and negro-english
; and, in short, it seemed clear that
something unusual was going forward.

What the deuce is up ? " thought Gerard ;
'' I may as well see what it's all about ; " and
putting on one or two articles of attire, he
opened his door and went out into the
passage. Here he met his friend Pedro,
rushing about in a state of great excitement,
from whom he gathered that something
or other had happened in connection
with "they dam Indians." Passing on to
the hall he found the master of the house
himself, fully attired in riding costume, and
with a couple of revolvers buckled round
his waist. He was giving orders in rapid
succession to various more or less excited
individuals, but appeared himself to be
perfectly cool and collected.

"Ah, Mr Courtenay," he exclaimed, on
catching sight of Gerard, "I'm afraid all
this noise has disturbed you. I'm sorry to
say they have just brought in one of my

herdsmen, who has been shot at and badly
wounded by some of these ruffians who have
been hanging about here lately ; and we
must go after them at once, and try and
clear them out of the place, or we shall
have no end of trouble with them. But
I'm very sorry you have been disturbed,
and I should think you had better go to
bed again."

But mayn't I come with you ? " asked
Gerard, thinking that an expedition of this
sort would be a new and unlooked-for excitement
; " I should like it of all things."

"Well, I think you had much better not,"
replied Mr Montenello. " I hardly suppose
these fellows will show fight, but there seem
to be a good many of them, and it is just
possible that we may exchange a shot or
two. It would never do, you know, for
you to get hurt while under my charge. I
don't know what Sir Henry Turner would
say, and your friends in England too, for the
matter of that."

Oh, pray don't think of that ! " exclaimed
Gerard, determined not to miss such a chance.

I can look after myself, I assure you, and I
shouldn't at all like to be left behind. I
don't suppose I'm the least likely to get shot ;
and anyhow, I'm quite ready to take my
chance, if you don't mind my coming with

you."

"Well, I don't suppose there will be any
very great danger," replied Mr Montenello,
smiling at Gerard's keenness, " and if you are
determined to come, so be it. You can have
the horse you rode yesterday, if you like.
But we shall be off in about ten minutes, so
you must look sharp."

"I'll be ready in five minutes," returned
Gerard, delighted at the prospect ; and he
hurried back to his room, where he proceeded
to get as quickly as possible into his riding
things. While so occupied, a cup of coffee
and some biscuits were brought to him by
Pedro, and before the ten minutes were over
he was quite ready to start.

"You may as well put this on," said
Mr Montenello, handing him a belt with a
revolver and cartridges; "not that I think
there is much chance of your having to use

VOL. III. B

it, but there is no harm in being armed on an
occasion like this. Now then, if you are ready
we will be off."

The open space in front of the door presented
an animated appearance. Some fifteen
or twenty horsemen were now assembled,
tightening girths, adjusting arms, and getting
everything in readiness for a march. Groups
of excited negroes and mulattoes were clustered
about, some brandishing torches or lanterns,
and others assisting, or pretending to
assist, in the preparations for departure, but
one and all chattering at the top of their
voices, and rather impeding than accelerating
matters by their well-meant officiousness.
The appearance of Mr Montenello at the
door, however, had the effect of producing
a certain amount of order, and silencing the
cackle of human voices. The mounted men,
who had evidently undergone some rough and
ready process of drilling, fell into a line in fair
order ; while one or two, who appeared to be
in a position of trust and authority, rode out
to the front to salute and take the orders of
their chief

Juan," said Mr Montenello in Spanish,
addressing a grizzled, weather-beaten veteran,
take four men with you, and ride straightaway
to Diego's ranch. If you see any number
of men hanging about there, send a man
back at once to report, and halt well out of
shot of the ranch, under cover if you can. We
shall not be far behind, and shall soon catch
you up."

The old man inclined his head with a sort
of military salute, and turning round to his
comrades, called four men by name, who atone
set spurs to their horses, and sprang out
to the front with a smartness that would
have done credit to any dragoons. Their
leader wheeled sharply to the right, and the
whole went off at a gallop, and were soon
lost to view in the still dim and uncertain
light.

Now then," said Mr Montenello, turning
to Gerard, "if you will mount, we will be off.
Here's your horse," as a negro led up the
same animal that Gerard had ridden the day
before, " and you'll find her carry you just as
well as yesterday." And mounting his own.

a powerful iron-grey that would have aroused
pangs of envy in the breast of a " squadron
leader" of the Greys, he moved out of the line,
now sitting " at attention," and addressed to
them a few words in Spanish. His observations
were received with a murmur of approval
; and, falling into a rough formation of
half-sections," the little band moved off at
a brisk trot, headed by their leader and
Gerard.

They soon cleared the confines of the
hacienda, and were out upon the vast expanse
of the llanos. It was yet barely
daybreak, and for the moment it seemed
darker than ever. But a golden streak
of light was slowly but surely creeping
up in the east, and in a few minutes
more the dark vault of heaven was illumined
by the first rays of a tropical sunrise.
The outlines of trees and shrubs began to
show distinctly against the sky; birds began
to chirp and twitter in the grass ; and
the world had once more woke up into
life.

Gerard felt a strange exhilaration stealing

over him as lie rode along in the fresh
morning air. The novelty of the situation,
the wildness of the surroundings, and the
general uncertainty of what was going to
happen next, to say nothing of the ever-increasing
puzzle as to the connection between
his Florence, far away in Wealdshire,
and the mysterious man in whose company
he found himself, imparted a tinge of romance
to his position that, to his somewhat imaginative
temperament, was very fascinating. The
little chestnut stepped out gaily under him,
as if the journey of the preceding day lead
been nothing more than ordinary exercise ;
the tramp of horses and the jingle of bridles
and spurs around him had a cheery and
exhilarating effect ; and altogether he felt
in high spirits.

They trotted sharply on for some time in
silence, along a sort of half-defined grassy
track. Gradually the sky grew lighter and
lighter, and the outlines of the vast plain
spread out clearly before them. The blue
ridges of the distant mountains began to
show themselves out of a curtain of mist;

here and there a sheet of water shone out
in the rays of the sun, now well above the
horizon; and everything looked fresh and
bright. Strings of clamorous wildfowl passed
over their heads, returning from their nocturnal
feeding-grounds to the reedy pools
which formed their resting-place during the
day, and making Gerard's mouth water fora
shot. Herds of cattle appeared every now
and then in the distance, with a mounted
herdsman or two hovering about them ; but
as yet there was no sign of anything like
a body of men, either friendly or otherwise.


They had travelled for about six miles
without drawing rein, when a man on horseback
was observed in the distance moving
towards them at a gallop. The little column,
at a word from their chief, quickened their
pace, and in a few moments the solitary
horseman was recognised as one of the advanced
guard. As he drew near, Mr Montenello
rode forward to meet him, making
a sign to the remainder to halt. The colloquy
did not last long, and the messenger

wheeled about and galloped back in the
direction from whence he had come.

"They have seen a few suspicious-looking
men hanging about near the ranch," said
Mr Montenello to Gerard as he rejoined
him, "but not in any strength. We had
better get on, however;" and addressing a
few words in Spanish to the little troop,
they set out again at a brisk pace. Another
couple of miles brought them to the ranch,
now a roofless ruin, but still affording a
certain amount of shelter behind its broken-down
walls. Here they found the advanced
guard, who had dismounted, and were smoking
and lounging by their horses, with the
exception of one man, who was posted asa
sentinel about a couple of hundred yards
away.

We will stop here a bit," said Mr Montenello
to Gerard, and gave the order to
dismount.

In another moment the party had filed
into the enclosure ; the men had dismounted,
and were easing girths, looking about for
water for their horses, or lighting the inevitable

cigarette for themselves ; and the
usually deserted ranch assumed the appearance
of a cavalry piquet.

Mr Montenello, who had been the last to
dismount, was now holding a conference with
old Juan, the commander of the advanced
guard, and with another herdsman who had
just turned up, and who, from his excited
manner and profuse gesticulations, appeared
to have something of importance to communicate.
The chief, however, did not
appear to be proportionately impressed, and
exhibited a coolness and indifference that
presented a great contrast to the excited
demeanour of his companions. Having at
length apparently obtained all the information
that it was in the power of these
worthies to communicate, he called up three
or four of the leading men of his party, and
gave them some orders in Spanish. And
then, turning to Gerard, —

I am afraid," he said, " that we shall
have to go on, and that this may be rather
a long business. The fact is, these fellows,
half of whom are Indians and the rest disbanded

soldiers, who are almost the greater
ruffians of the two, have driven off a number
of our cattle, and we are bound to go after
them ; but they seem to have got some miles
ahead, and have just left one or two behind
to watch us. I really think you had better
go back, for we may very possibly have to
camp out, and at this damp season it is apt
to be dangerous if you are not used to it.
I can send a man back with you, and you
can ride quietly home."

This proposal, however, did not at all
meet Gerard's views. It would never do,
he thought, to go home in this ignominious
way, just as there seemed a chance of something
like a real adventure.

"If you don't mind," he said, "I had
much rather stay with you and see it out.
I can stand roughing it pretty well, I assure
you ; and if I shan't be in the way, I should
like very much to go on."

Very well," replied Mr Montenello, evidently
not displeased at Gerard's enthusiasm,
you must do as you think best. Now then,
we must be off again." And in another

minute or two they had remounted, and the
deserted ranch was left once more to the
lizards and chameleons that were its usual
inhabitants.

They now proceeded with greater precautions,
as if in the neighbourhood of an
enemy. Old Juan and his advanced guard
were again sent on ahead, and a single horseman
was detached as a scout on each flank.
Not that there appeared much danger of the
appearance of the enemy without due warning
; for except in one direction, the llanos
stretched away all round, and a horseman or
any similar object could easily be seen at a
considerable distance. Straight in front of
them, however, about three or four miles
away, was some broken ground, with a few
clumps of trees, beyond which the ground
gradually rose till it joined on to a low-lying
spur of the hills that bounded one side of the
plain. It was in this direction that the men
seen by the advanced guard had retired, and
here, if taken proper advantage of, there was
cover for any number of men.

They had arrived within a quarter of a mile

of the first clump of trees, round wliicli Juan
and his men were circling at a gallop, the
remainder slackening their pace, and keeping
a good look-out on all sides, when all of a
sudden some five or six strange horsemen
rose up, as it were, out of the ground about
five hundred yards to the right, and galloped
away at full speed towards the hills. A yell
arose from the troop, and half-a-dozen men
dashed forward in pursuit. " Steady ! " shouted
Mr Montenello in a tone of command, spurring
his horse to the front, and restraining
the others. With a wave of his hand he
signalled to one of his lieutenants to go forward
with those who had already started,
and called to the remainder to follow him.
Directing his course slightly to the left, and
closely followed by Gerard, he led the way in
what seemed rather a contrary direction from
that which the fugitives had taken. ^' We
must get round them," he explained to Gerard,
who was riding close at his side ; " they can't
go far that way because of the rough ground,
and our men will turn them to us. We are
sure to catch them."

It soon became apparent that Mr Monte-mello
was right. The strange horsemen had
been concealed in one of those curious ravines
or fissures that are not uncommon in the
volcanic soil of the llanos, and were now
straining every nerve to reach the shelter
of the wooded spur in front. To reach this,
however, they would have to cross a deeper
and more precipitous ravine, which now
made itself apparent, and which, while it
might possibly have been crossed in safety
by horses carefully picking their way at a
foot's pace, would have been a most dangerous
if not impassable obstacle to men going
at full gallop. Their pursuers were not far
behind ; and after hesitating for a moment,
they apparently decided to avoid the ravine,
and make for the friendly woods by a longer
detour across the plain ; thus, however, bringing
themselves between two fires, as it were,
and decreasing their distance from the party
led by Mr Montenello. This was exactly
what the latter had expected. " I know the
ground better than they do," he said with a
smile to Gerard, touching his horse lightly

with the spur, " and they can't well get away
from us."

The chase had now lasted some seven or
eight minutes. Mr Montenello and Gerard,
being better mounted than the majority of
their followers, were some fifty yards ahead,
and a somewhat lengthening "tail" showed
that the pace was beginning to tell. The
wooded slopes of the hillside were now not
far distant, and a few minutes more would
settle the question. The fugitives were
making for a point some seven or eight
hundred yards away, where a rocky opening
seemed to indicate a sort of rough road up
into the higher ground. But a little short
of this point was a wooded promontory or
spit of vegetation which ran out into the
plain, and which had to be passed by Mr
Montenello and his party in order to cutoff
the retreat of the .others. They were
flying along about a hundred yards from
the wood when suddenly a puff of smoke,
followed by two or three sharp reports,
floated out from the bushes, and Gerard,
who was a little way behind Mr Montenello,

saw, to his horror, that he swerved in his
saddle, and then bent slowly forward over
his horse's neck.

Good God, he's shot ! " exclaimed Gerard,
putting spurs to his horse, and closely followed
by two or three of those behind who had
also witnessed the occurrence. It was evident
that their leader had lost all command
over his horse, which, under these circumstances,
might very probably have bolted
with him. But the pace had been very
severe, and the animal gradually subsided
into a trot. In another moment an active
young llanero had caught him by the bridle,
and half-a-dozen arms where stretched out to
help their chief to the ground.

They lifted him gently down, and laid
him tenderly on the grass. It was not a
moment too soon, for his face had turned
a deadly white, and they had scarcely got
him clear of his horse when he closed his
eyes and fainted away. Old Juan threw
himself on the ground, and pillowed his
master's head on his knees.

Madre de Dios ! " exclaimed the old man.

in tones of anguish, the tears rolling down
his weather-beaten cheeks, "he is dying.
Holy Virgin, help us ! what shall we do "? "

Caramba I don't crowd on him so ! " exclaimed
an English voice ; and looking up,
Gerard recognised his friend Tom Lockyer,
who had not formed one of the original
expedition, but who, as it subsequently
appeared, had followed with another man
on his own account. " Give him some air
for Heaven's sake, or he won't be able to
breathe." And by dint of entreaties and
the use of a certain amount of force, he
succeeded in clearing a space round the
wounded man. "Now get some water, and
sprinkle it on his face," he continued, as he
took his master's hand in his, and felt the
scarcely throbbing pulse. "He'll come to,
never fear ; it's only just a swoon."

But his face belied his words ; and Gerard's
eye, following his, caught sight of a dark
spot of blood on Mr Montenello's breast,
that seemed to grow larger and larger, and
spoke of a serious wound.

"Now, Ramon," continued Tom, who had

assumed the direction of affairs, addressing
the young man who had brought the water,
the same who had stopped Mr Montenello's
horse, — " you gallop home as fast as you can,
and bring up the doctor. We will try and
get the Senhor down part of the way towards
home, and meet you. And make
Sancho yoke his team to the new waggon,
and come up as quick as he can ; we shall
get him home better that way."

The young man nodded assent, and vaulting
on to his horse, darted away like an
arrow across the plain. All eyes were now
turned upon the motionless form of Mr
Montenello. He still lay in a swoon, the
noble features rigid as in death, and the
faint beating of his heart alone showing
that life was not extinct. Old Juan from
time to time tenderly moistened his face
with water, while Tom held the almost
pulseless hand, watching for the slightest
return of animation. They had opened his
shirt, and the wound was now apparent — a
dark, hideous spot on the right breast. There
was little external bleeding ; " a bad sign that,'

murmured Tom, shaking his head ; and it was
evident that nothing further could be done at
the moment.

In the mean time some ten or twelve of
the band had detached themselves from the
others and struck off in the direction of the
hills, in the hope of overtaking and wreaking
vengeance on the cowardly marauders. But
far too great a start had been allowed ; and
before long the detachment was seen returning
slowly from the hill-side, in the dejected
manner peculiar to men who have failed in
their enterprise.

An hour passed away, slowly and sadly,
during the greater part of which Mr Montenello
lay in a deadly stupor. At last,
however, some symptoms of returning consciousness
began to manifest themselves. He
slowly opened his eyes, gave one or two
gasps, and looked around in a dazed manner,
like a man in a dream. He tried to raise
his head, and his lips moved, as if in an
attempt to speak; but the effort was too
much for him, and he sank back again into

VOL. III. F

Juan's arms, with a long-drawn sigh that was
painful to hear.

"We must try and move him," said Tom,
decisively, looking round on the melancholy
group ; " if we don't get the doctor to him
soon, it will be all up with him. We must
just carry him till we meet the waggon, and
then see what the doctor says."

During the long weary wait, the men
had busied themselves, under Tom's direction,
in constructing a rough sort of litter
out of girths and horse-furniture. This was
now placed on the ground by the side of
their chief, who was tenderly lifted into it.
Six men, kneeling down, three on each side,
slowly and carefully raised the litter on to
their shoulders, and moved off with it at a
foot's pace. The movement did not appear
to cause Mr Montenello pain, and he lay
with half-closed eyes without showing any
further uneasiness.

Slowly the melancholy cavalcade moved
across the plain, the litter with the wounded
man and the led horses of the bearers giving
a funereal appearance to the little band that

but an hour or two before bad swept along
in all the eager pride of horsemanship.
Every eye was anxiously strained in the
direction of the hacienda; and by the time
they had advanced about a mile, some dark
specks were seen in the distance, which,
moving at a rapid pace, were soon made out
to be a wheeled vehicle accompanied by
three or four men on horseback. "Thank
God, here they come ! " ejaculated Tom, a
sentiment that was heartily echoed by the
rest; "we may as well stop now, and wait
till they come up to us."

It was not long before the forms of the
advancing party were clearly made out, and
in a few minutes more they were close at
hand. They came up, waggon and all, at
full gallop ; and even in the distress and
anxiety of the moment, Gerard could not
help being struck by the way in which the
driver of the waggon, a stalwart young negro,
handled his team of fine mules, behind which
the light waggon bounded and jolted like a
shuttlecock over the uneven grassy surface
of the savannah. A bed had been placed

in the waggon ; and on this, by the direction
of the doctor, the almost unconscious
form of Mr Montenello was gently laid.
The party formed round the waggon as an
escort, and at a foot's pace the sad procession
set out once more towards home.

THE SHADOW OF DEATH.

It was getting on for midnight. Gerard
was sitting alone in the little study or
smoking-room, where lie lead been, in fact,
for the last three or four hours. They had
brought him some supper, which, after the
fatigue and excitement of the day, was very
acceptable; and since then he had been sitting
waiting, in a state of vague and fearful
expectation, for the announcement that
he knew must shortly come. For there could
be no doubt about it — Mr Montenello was
dying. The doctor, an intelligent young
Englishman, had made a thorough examination
of the wound as soon as they got him
home, and had pronounced it to be of the
most serious description. The bullet had
penetrated the right lung and lodged itself
under the shoulder-blade, in a locality from

which it would have been most dangerous,
if not impossible, to attempt to remove it.
There had been great internal haemorrhage,
and were this to come on again, which was
only too probable, it was barely possible that
the wounded man could survive. Such was
the melancholy bulletin that had been announced
to Gerard, Pedro, Diadems, and
those immediately about Mr Montenello's
person, and from them caught up and circulated
to a rapidly increasing crowd that was
clustering round every corner of the hacienda,
struggling with and jostling one another
for news of their beloved master, and
only restrained by the adjurations and threats
of those about him, backed by the authority
of the doctor, who pronounced that perfect
quiet was the only chance, from forcing their
way into the building itself.

Gerard had remained for a time in the
wounded man's room, until advised by the
doctor to retire. He was now sitting crouched
up in a lounging chair, gazing in a dreamy
sort of way, by the light of a solitary lamp,
at the portrait of Florence on the table, and

thinking to what an extraordinary chain of
events his expedition to San Fernando had
led. He had been brought into contact, by
the merest accident, with this mysterious
friend of Florence's, from whom it had seemed
highly probable that he would be able to
obtain some information respecting her ; and
now the angel of death was about to step
in between them. It was all very strange and
terrible ; and the more he thought of it, the
more extraordinary his position appeared to
him.

As he sat there in a half-stupefied state,
wondering what was going to be the end
of it all, and occasionally thinking how he
should get back to San Fernando, he heard
a sudden murmur of voices in the passage
outside, and fancied he distinguished his own
name. In another moment the door opened,
and the doctor looked in and beckoned to him
to come out. " He wants to see you," he
whispered to Gerard; "he is rather better
just now, but I am afraid it will not befor
long."
• Tom was on guard outside Mr Montenello's

room, and softly opened the door as Gerard
approached. The room was dimly lighted
by a shaded lamp, and the only figure
discernible at first was that of an old priest,
who was sitting by the bedside. On seeing
Gerard enter, he rose from his seat,
and beckoned to him to take his place. He
then glided noiselessly from the room, and
Gerard was left alone in the chamber of death.

For a moment or two there was a dead
silence. Gerard could now distinguish the
outlines of the wounded man's head, as he
lay propped up by pillows, with one hand
restlessly wandering over the bed-clothes.
He was on the point of saying something,
when Mr Montenello spoke.

*^ Young man," he said, in a voice that
thrilled Gerard through and through, " I
have not many hours to live, and I have
something of importance to say to you before
I die."

Gerard tried to speak, but his tongue
seemed to cleave to his lips. He could
only bow assent, and Mr Montenello continued.


*' You have spoken of Miss Florence
Graham, and of having made her acquaintance
in England. In fact," he went on,
"I believe I am not wrong in supposing
that you have some feelings of attachment
for her?"

Gerard gave a start. Then she had spoken
about him, and he was going to hear something
about her after all. In spite of the
overpowering sadness of the occasion, he
was unable to repress a secret feeling of
exultation. He murmured something in
reply, scarcely knowing what he said, and
Mr Montenello continued.

I daresay," he said, speaking slowly and
painfully, but yet with perfect distinctness,
*you may have thought it strange that Miss
Graham should be living at Netherdean with
Mr Baldwin, and may have wondered who
she was ? "

Gerard again made an almost inaudible
response. It was coming, after all, and he
was now to hear the story of Florence's
life.

"It is perhaps as well that you should

know this at once," resumed Mr Montenello.
Florence Graham is my daughter."

Somehow, this announcement did not take
Gerard altogether by surprise. Although the
idea had previously occurred to him, but had
been dismissed as scarcely worth considering,
the fact that it really was so did. not produce
any very great effect upon him, and it seemed
to him, for the moment, to be merely a
matter of detail. There was something more
to come, he felt sure; and for this he waited
with a painful, feverish anxiety.

And yet," continued Mr Montenello, " her
name is not really Graham. Neither is it
Montenello ; for, as I need hardly tell you, I
am an Englishman, and have no foreign blood
in me whatever.

I am going to tell you something," the
dying man went on, fixing his eyes steadfastly
on Gerard, " that will probably astonish
you very much, and will astonish a good many
others still more."

Gerard felt a strange, unaccountable sort of
shiver stealing over him. He said nothing,
but clasped his hands nervously together.

trying to steel himself for what might be to
come. There was a pause, during which he
dared not look up, and then Mr Montenello
resumed.

"You were probably rather surprised," he
said, in slow, deliberate accents, " to find Joe
Diadems here. For I believe you know
Diadems ? In fact, you have probably recognised
me as the person who spoke to you
one afternoon at Easthampton, and you may
perhaps have connected Diadems' disappearance
with me ? "

Gerard nodded assent, wondering what this
was going to lead to. He did not quite seethe
connection as yet.

"You know Easthampton pretty well, I daresay.
And you are probably well acquainted
with the Narborough family. In fact, I believe
there is some sort of connection between
you, is there not ? "

"Yes," replied Gerard, in a voice that
sounded to himself strangely hoarse and unnatural,
" yes, I have known them all my life,
more or less, and we are connected in a sort of
way by marriage."

"Just SO," replied Mr Montenello, slowly
and calmly. " You are no doubt, then, well
acquainted with the circumstances under
which the present Lord Narborough's father
succeeded to the title ?

You are aware," he went on, as if taking
Gerard's assent for granted, " that the brother
of the late Lord Narborough, uncle to the
present peer, left England when quite a
young man, and never came back again,
having, it seems, been killed on board a
river steamer in America ? "

Gerard silently assented. It seemed to
him that some strange revelation was impending,
and he sat with parted lips and
attention strained to the utmost.

I suppose," continued the dying man,
that it was proved beyond a doubt that
this Lord Narborough was killed in the way
I have mentioned ? "

There was a dead silence, during which
Gerard could hear the beating of his own
heart, and feel the perspiration rolling down
his face. His brain seemed to reel ; he could
not think, and could only listen.

And yet," continued Mr Montenello,
more as if soliloquising than as if addressing
another person, "we are all of us liable
to be deceived sometimes. Supposing that
there had been some mistake after all, and
that this Lord Narborough had not really
been killed, as was imagined ?

"Supposing, for instance," he went on,
that he had made up his mind, for reasons
of his own, to sacrifice his title and position
in England, and live an obscure and unknown
life in foreign countries, working as
an ordinary mechanic, and trusting to this
own exertions to procure a livelihood.

Supposing, as after all is not altogether
impossible, that he had come to the con-conclusion
that the only way to accomplish
his purpose would be to altogether destroy
his identity, and, regardless of the feelings
of his family and friends, let it be imagined
that he was dead, so that the title might
pass to his brother, and he himself be forgotten.


Supposing that by some means or other
he had ascertained that he was being sought

out and tracked — a circumstance that in
itself might be apt to increase his desire to
disappear from the world — and that he had
in consequence determined to baffle once for
all those who were in search of him.

Supposing that by a curious coincidence
he had come across a young man who resembled
him in every respect to a remarkable
degree, and with whom he had become
so intimate as to impart to him his real
history; and that the idea had occurred to
him to supply his friend with certain articles
which, if found, would be recognised as his
property, and then start him off upon a
journey where he would be easily followed
up to a certain point, after which he was
to disappear altogether, in such a manner
that it would be next to impossible to
obtain any further trace of him.

And supposing, last of all," continued Mr
Montenello, in a voice that seemed to Gerard
like that of a man speaking from the grave,
'' that Providence, which we do not always
take sufficiently into account, had so willed
it, and so assisted his design, that the man

whom he had employed to personate him
should really have been killed, and that inconsequence
it should have gone forth to the
world in an indisputable manner that Lord
Narborough had met with a violent death;
why then, there would be nothing to prevent
him from carrying out his scheme to any
extent. He might have assumed another
name, and become an American citizen ; he
might have amassed money, and devoted himself
to mercantile enterprise; or he might,
perhaps, have turned his ideas to colonisation,
and with a lingering love for English habits
and customs, have endeavoured to found a
sort of settlement in some comparatively unknown
country, with the idea of opening up
a new field for English emigrants. Some
place like Venezuela, for instance"

''My Grot!" ejaculated Gerard, sinking on
his knees by the bedside, as the truth flashed
across him with a vividness that was almost
overpowering, " you don't mean to say "

Yes," replied Mr Montenello, in a calm
voice, that contrasted strangely with Gerard's
emotion ; " I see you understand me. Yes,

young man, I am Francis Lord Narborough ;
and it is strange that it should be to you
that I should first reveal my secret. But it
is all for the best, perhaps. Ah ! no wonder
you are astonished ; it is a curious story,
certainly."

For Gerard, his face buried in his hands,
was huddled up on his knees against the bed,
completely overcome by the tremendous and
unexpected revelation that had just been
made to him, and seemed utterly incapable
of speech or thought.

You must collect yourself, my boy," said
Mr Montenello, in a kind and sympathetic
voice, that now began to tremble through
weakness; "for I have something more to
say to you before I die. Ah ! you are better
now. Just reach me that glass, for I am
beginning to feel weaker, and if this bleeding
comes on again, I am done for."

Now," he continued, in a firmer voice,
having drunk the contents of the glass that
Gerard handed to him, " you know whom
you are speaking to, and you will understand
better what I have to say. By the way, I

presume you believe what I have told you,
and do not imagine that I am romancing.
Stop," as Gerard shook his head in deprecation
of the idea. '^ It is just as well that
you should see some proofs. Take those
keys," pointing to a bunch on a table, " and
unlock that despatch-box in the corner. It
is the key with an ivory label. That's it.
Now then, bring me one or two of those
packets of letters, and I will show you who
they are from."

Gerard obeyed his orders implicitly. It was
an old-fashioned morocco despatch-box, with
no name or initials outside. But he started ashe
opened it ; for, among the neatly-arranged
papers that lay at the top, he observed a
packet of letters, the handwriting of which,
although the ink was yellow with age, seemed
strangely familiar to him. Yes, there could
be no doubt about it ; the form of the letters
and the general style of writing was somewhat
altered, but there were certain characteristics
that were unmistakable. It was the handwriting
of his own father.

Let me see that packet," said Mr Mon-vol.
