tenello. " All ! you ought to know that
writing, for it is your father's. That is to
say, I take it for granted that you are a son
of Charles Courtenay, — he and I were great
friends at that time, and you may possibly
have heard him speak of me. You may
read them, if you like ; they relate to matters
that are long dead and buried, but I daresay
he might remember them yet. There are a
few there from my mother, whom of course
you cannot remember, and one or two from
my sister Julia, who is still alive. The other
papers will be of no interest to you, but they
may be all useful in evidence."

Gerard turned over the papers with a
feeling of wonder and awe. There were
letters of all sorts, some addressed to " The
Earl of Narborough," at various places, in
England as well as abroad, and others without
envelopes, and in all sorts of writing, now
grown dim and indistinct with age. Glancing
into the box, he could see various odds and
ends, including an old banker's pass-book,
an almanac of the year 1831, and the draft
of the lease of a farm on the Easthampton

property. They all seemed to liii like relics
from the tomb, and he could hardly believe
that the whole thing was not a dream.

I daresay you will believe what 1 have
told you," resumed Mr Montenello ; *' though,
after all, it does not much matter, for there
are plenty of proofs in Baldwin's keeping.
And now, young man, my time is short, and
there is another matter about which I wish
to speak to you. I have told you that the
young lady you know as Florence Graham
is my daughter ; and her real name therefore
is, of course. Lady Florence Middleton ; for
though she never knew her mother," and
here his voice trembled with weakness and
emotion, "our marriage was perfectly legal
and orthodox, and I have taken care that
there shall be abundant proof of it. She
has long since gone to her rest, and I shall
soon join her. It would be too long a story
now to tell you the history of my life, even
if I had strength for it. But you must
understand that from the first I had an
idea of never returning to England as Lord
Narborough. It was a foolish fancy of mine,

no doubt; and if circumstances had turned
out otherwise, I might very likely have
thought better of it. But when my poor
friend was killed, and I found how readily
the story of my own death was believed, I
felt as if there were a sort of judgment in
it, and I made a vow that I would never
divulge my secret. It has often seemed tome
very remarkable that the lawyers and
others were so easily deceived. You may
possibly have heard that one of the most
important points was considered to be the
finding of certain property of mine in his
possession, and I have hinted to you how I
managed this. It was perfectly simple and
easy, and perhaps that was the very reason
they never thought of it."

There was a pause. Mr Montenello seemed
to grow weaker, and his voice sounded faint
and indistinct. Gerard started up, and made
a movement towards the door, with the idea
of summoning the doctor. But the dong
man, appearing to divine his intention,
arrested him by a motion of his hand.
Stop," he said, in somewhat firmer accents,

I have not finished all I have to say to
you. Fill that glass again from the bottle,
and give it to me. And now, attend, for
I feel that my strength will not last much
longer."

I have suffered in various ways," he went
on, " for what I have long since known to
have been a foolish and ill-considered step ;
but my worst punishment has been as regards
my child, and the necessity that has arisen
for keeping her real name and position a
secret. This has been a source of bitter
distress to me, particularly during the last
few years. I have seen her now and then,
as you know, and it has been a terrible thing
for me to think that I have been the means
of depriving her of her name, her family, and
everything that ought to have been hers.
And on her account, therefore, I am thankful
to die ; for my death will at once clear
everything up, and place her in her true
position.

"And now, young man, what am I to say
to you ? Is it really and truly the case that
you love my daughter ? "

Gerard essayed to speak, but the words at
first refused to come. " God knows I do," at
last lie half whispered, in husky accents ; " I
have loved her ever since I first spoke to
her."

Mr Montenello looked at him long and
earnestly, as if he would read his thoughts.
I don't know why I should not believe you,"
he said, at length, " and you appear to me to
be speaking the truth. I need hardly say,
without any disparagement to you, that if
I could have had my choice, I might have
looked elsewhere for a husband for her. But
that has been impossible, and I must take the
consequences of my own actions. It was of
course only to be expected that she should
fall in love with some one who would be a
perfect stranger to me, and I have no one to
blame but myself."

But," interrupted Gerard, trembling with
excitement, " I don't know — I mean, I'm
not sure — do you really thinks she cares forme?"


Why should you ask me ? " returned Mr
Montenello, with a tinge of bitterness in his

voice. '' I have never dared reveal myself to
her, and how should I claim her confidence ?
I have only been to her as a friend, — perhaps
something more than an ordinary one, — and
as a child she has talked to me freely about
many things ; but what right have I had to
inquire into the secrets of her heart ? No ; all
I have heard about you has been through
Baldwin, whom she has naturally looked upon
much more as a father than on me ; and he
appears, rightly or wrongly, to have formed
an idea that she had — well, that she had been
thinking about a young fellow of the name
of Courtenay, who had come down therefor
a short time, and then suddenly disappeared."


Gerard felt a wild and indescribable joy
taking possession of him. He said nothing,
however, and Mr Montenello went on.

It was a curious thing that I should meet
you at Easthampton. I had not been near
the place for many years, and was seized with
a sudden desire to see it once more. It was
strange, too, that I should come across Did-duns,
the only man who was in the least

likely to recognise me ; but he knew me at
once, poor old fellow, and I felt then that
it was not safe to leave him there. It
is strange, very strange, that I should again
meet you in this way, and that you should be
with me in my last hours. Stop," he continued,
as Gerard was about to speak ; " you
must not suppose that I am going to assure
you of my child's love, that would be going
rather too far. No ; I have no right to speak
on her account, and you will have to make
your own way with her. But I believe you
to be an honourable young fellow, and indeed
I can hardly think that the son of a man who
was once one of my greatest friends could be
otherwise. I know something about you, you
see ; I have heard of your being private secretary
to Lord Kavendale, and of your being
occasionally at Easthampton; but I certainly
never expected to see you here.

"You will see my child after I am
gone," he went on, after a pause, during
which his breathing seemed to grow more
painful and difficult. " You will tell her
that I revealed myself to you ; she will

soon know all about it, poor girl, for the
moment my death is known to have taken
place, Baldwin is to tell her everything. A
telegraphic message will go to him, which
is all ready prepared, and which will be
found with my papers. Father Domenico
will see to this. But there is something
that I should like her to have direct from
me," and he drew a small packet from
under his pillow. "Now, young man, can
I trust you to take this straight to her,
and tell her that you brought it from her
father's deathbed ? "

Gerard trembled with mixed feelings of
awe and delight as he took the precious
packet in his hands. It was addressed to
"The Lady Florence Middleton," and it
seemed to him like a passport or sacred
credential to her whom he loved best in
the world.

You can trust me, indeed you can ! "
he exclaimed. " And you will not forbid
me to — to try and win her love ? "

But the dying man did not answer. He
fixed his eyes on Gerard with a glassy stare,

and his lips moved as if to speak, but no
sound issued from them, and a deadly pallor
overspread his face. With a cry of horror,
Gerard sprang to the door, and called to
the doctor and the others outside to came
in. They raised his head, and the doctor
forced some restorative into his mouth.
His pulse is nearly gone," he muttered ;
"the end cannot be far off." And the
melancholy party grouped themselves around
to await the closing scene.

But the end was not to be just yet. By
degrees a slight flush of colour returned to
the pale cheeks, and the pulse grew a little
stronger. He muttered some words indistinctly,
and his eyes wandered as if in
search of some one. Gerard was about to
speak to him, but the doctor stopped him.
"You must leave him alone," he whispered;
I fear there is no hope ; but the only
shadow of a chance is in his being perfectly
quiet." So Gerard sat down near the head
of the bed, out of sight of the dying man,
and abandoned himself to his own reflections.


So the hours wore on. The stillness of
the tropical night was only broken by the
subdued and indistinct murmuring of the
crowd outside, or by an occasional movement
in the chamber of death. Now and
then the door would be gently opened, and
one of the mourners would steal out, to be
replaced by some other, anxious for a look
at their beloved master. One figure alone
retained its position at the foot of the bed,
without moving — an old man, sitting crouched
on a low stool, his face buried in his
hands, and his whole appearance indicative
of the deepest grief. It was old Diadems,
who had crept into the room when Gerard
called the doctor, and had never left *it
since. Now and then he would slowly raise
his head, and look up in a scared and distracted
manner, and then with a low moan
resume his former attitude.

It was now about half past three. Gerard,
overcome by excitement and fatigue, was
dozing on one side of the bed, and the old
priest, who had just relieved the doctor, was
on the other. Suddenly, and as if by some

instinct, he awoke with a start, and bent over
the bed. Mr Montenello's eyes were wide
open, and a change had come over his face.
His eyes met Gerard's, and he smiled, with
lips parted, as if about to speak. Gerard
took his hand, which was lying outside the
bed-clothes, and the dying man's fingers
closed with a gentle pressure upon his. Then
his eyes wandered away in the direction of
the window, and a strange, unearthly look
of peace and happiness came over his face,
as if he saw something that gave him ineffable
pleasure. Gerard instinctively followed
his gaze. The day was beginning to
dawn, and a faint streak of light was just
visible in the horizon. A light breeze had
sprung up, and was whispering softly in the
creepers and trellis-work of the verandah outside
— a weird, mysterious sighing, the gentle
requiem of a departing soul. A bright smile
illumined the features of the dying man,
and his gaze was earnestly riveted in the
direction of the window. " Isidora, Isidora
mia," he murmured, in scarcely audible
accents. But it was a last effort. The

straining eyes grew dimmer and dimmer,
till it was apparent that the sight had departed
from them; Gerard felt the pressure
on his hand slowly relax ; and as
the morning star rose, solemnly, sweetly,
over the boundless llanos, there was a last,
long-drawn sigh, and the heavy head sank
down to rest.

It was all over. The world-worn bark
had at last passed into the harbour, and
the weary spirit had returned to God who
gave it.

Gerard fell on his knees by the bedside,
and buried his face in his hands. His head
swam round, his brain reeled, and he fell
into a kind of swoon. How long he knelt
there he knew not ; but he was roused by
a gentle touch on his shoulder, and looking
up, he perceived that he was surrounded by
a crowd of faces, among which was that of
the old priest, who was bending over him
with a look of intense kindness and sympathy.
"He is taken from us, my son," he
whispered in French, in grave and gentle
accents; "God's will be done. Let us pray

for this soul's repose." And kneeling down,
he offered up a solemn prayer for the dead.
*rand now," lie said, as he rose from his
knees, " we must leave him for the present ; "
and motioning to the others to follow him,
he led Gerard out of the room.

They passed slowly through the hall,
crowded with anxious and sorrowing figures,
and went out on to the steps, where a
strange sight presented itself. A swarm of
swarthy, upturned faces, just discernible in
the cold dawn, and lighted up here and
there by torches ; groups of women and even
young children sitting crouched and huddled
up in corners ; altogether some three or four
hundred souls, the entire population, in fact,
of the little settlement, who had been patiently
waiting during the weary hours of
the night, hoping against hope for more
favourable news. It was a weird and picturesque
scene, and well worthy of a painter.
As the familiar form of the padre appeared
at the door, there was a murmur and rustle
of expectation ; but his face told the tale
only too plainly ; and as he slowly raised his

hand and made the sign of the cross, there
rose up a cry of sorrow that went to Gerard's
very heart. It was a fitting and touching
tribute to the memory of him who was
gone, a suitable closing scene to a strange
and melancholy life.

HOME AGAIN.

How Gerard got back to San Fernando lie
never quite knew. He had indistinct recollections
of a conference with Father Domenico,
whom he found, to his surprise, to be well
acquainted with Mr Montenello's real history,
including the circumstances connected with
his marriage, at which time, it appeared, he
had become a convert to the Woman Catholic
religion. He resolved, therefore, to confide to
him the story of his attachment to Florence,
urging him, at the same time, not to send off
the telegraphic message that had been referred
to, but to let him be the bearer of the sad
intelligence to Florence in person. The old
priest, who showed much interest in Gerard's
story, consented, after some hesitation, to do
as he desired, and promised to take no steps
in the matter until he should hear from him

after this arrival in England. Then came a
vision of a hurried ride, in company with Tom
Lockyer, to Puerto Carrero, and of the sight of
the Government steamer in the bay, the captain
of which was manifesting his impatience by
the exhibition of various unintelligible signals
with flags and other maritime devices, and by
frantic blasts from his steam whistle. It was
late in the evening by the time he got onboard,
and the excitement of the little captain,
who had been lying-to for four hours or
more, and whose temper had not been thereby
improved, was decidedly impressive. But his
expostulations and reproaches were quite lost
upon Gerard, who climbed mechanically onboard,
like a man in a dream, and exhibited
no emotion or sense of shame whatever. He
sank down upon a seat on deck, his head
resting on his hand, where he remained for
some time after the steamer had got out of
sight of Puerto Carrero, absorbed in his own
thoughts, and apparently altogether unconscious
of the curious glances that were cast
at him, or the comments excited by his

haggard and scared appearance. As night
VOL. ni. H

came on, lie obeyed the captain's invitation to
go below, and excusing himself from appearing
at supper on the plea of feeling unwell,
lay down in his berth to rest. But, wearied
as he was, sleep was for a long time out of the
question ; and when at last he dropped off
into a broken and troubled state of semi-consciousness,
he was haunted by an ever-present
vision of the solemn scene through
which he had passed, and the calm, pale face
of Florence's father — for he could at present
think of him in no other way — was continually
before his eyes. It was only on
his return to Government House that he
was able to rouse himself a little and collect
his thoughts for what was before him.
The change back to the bustle and hurry
of civilisation was in itself a relief to him,
and helped to calm his mind. His first
step was to seek an interview with the
Governor, and communicate to him, under
promise of secrecy, the startling news of Mr
Montenello's death, and of his identity with
the long-forgotten Lord Narborough. But
he could not quite bring himself to reveal all

that had passed, and accounted for this being
in possession of the secret by its having
accidentally transpired that he was a distant
connection of the Narborough family.

Sir Henry was deeply interested in Gerard's
recital, and much touched by the strange story
of Mr Montenello's life. "You have played
a part in a most extraordinary romance," he
said, as they parted next day ; " nothing I
ever read in fiction beats this. But it quite
carries out my theory ; for, as I told you, I
always felt sure there was some mystery about
him, and that he was not what he professed
to be. I shall look out anxiously for an
account of the case in the papers, for of course
it will all come out now ; and in the meantime
you can trust to us to keep it dark.
Very glad to have made your acquaintance,
and I hope we shall meet again some day.
You must give a good account of us here at
home if they ask you any questions ; and
don't drop that precious charter overboard, or
Mr Mills will certainly commit suicide, and
the world will come to an end. Good-bye,
and good luck to you."

That's a nice, gentlemanlike young fellow,"
observed Sir Henry to his aide-de-camp, as
they stood together on the pier watching the
rapidly - receding steamer; "but, somehow
or other, I fancy he hasn't told us quite
everything. He must have found out something
more about this man, or he would
never have been in such a strange scared state
as he seems to be now. I shouldn't be at all
surprised if there were a young lady mixed
up with it somehow. ' Cherchez la femme
eh ? Anyhow, I'm pretty sure there's something
more behind. It's a strange story
anyway, and I shall be very curious to hear
the end of it.^'

We will pass over the details of Gerard's
return voyage to England in company with
his precious charter. It was perhaps just
as well for him, under the circumstances,
that he came in for a spell of unusually rough
and disagreeable weather, which, as he was
anything but a good sailor, necessitated his
keeping his berth for the greater part of
the first ten days ; and his thoughts being
thereby concentrated on his own personal

discomfort, were prevented from dwelling
upon the strange and terrible experience
through which he had just passed. Had it
not been for the precious letter, which he
fondly hoped might be the means of bringing
him nearer to Florence, and the superscription
of which he read and re-read a dozen times
a day, the whole thing would have seemed to
him a sort of nightmare. .About the tenth
day of the voyage he staggered on deck,
having at last got over his sea sickness, but
feeling as if all his strength had gone from
him, and scarcely capable of doing more shan
sit in a lounging chair and think. But even
then he could hardly collect his thoughts,
and found it impossible to prevent his mind
from wandering off to all sorts of trivial and
frivolous objects. There were, as it happened,
but few passengers of any kind, and certainly
none with whom he had any desire to associate
; and having, moreover, only appeared
when the voyage was half over, he was
naturally out of the little coteries that had
already been formed, and found himself almost
entirely isolated, a position which, in his

present state of mind, was far from being
unacceptable. The remainder of the voyage
Alas fine ; and as they got nearer home, he
felt himself gradually steadying down, and
feeling more composed both in mind and
body. For the last two or three days, indeed,
he quite enjoyed being at sea ; and, much ashe
longed to see Florence again, and speak to
her as he now felt he might, there was a
certain undefinable sense of shrinking from
the coming back once more into the world,
and the severing of the last link that seemed
to connect him with the Yen ezu elan hacienda
and its strange story. It was with curiously
mixed feelings, therefore, that he encountered
the first well-known signs of proximity to old
England ; and his last night on board was
passed in a broken and feverish slumber.
Towards morning, however, he at last dropped
into a sound and dreamless sleep, from which
he was aroused by an unwonted trampling
overhead, the blowing of steam whistles,
shouting of orders, and other demonstrations
associated with the approach to port ; and,
sitting up in his berth, he became conscious

of that gentle, gliding motion which brings
such unspeakable relief to sea-sick voyagers,
and betokens the entry into smooth water.
He started up, feeling that the time had
come for him to collect all his energies ; and,
going on deck, found that they were already
in the Southampton Water. In another hour
or two they were alongside the pier.

Gerard's first object was to get to the
General Enquiry Office without a moment's
delay, and then be free to go in search of
Florence. In his capacity of a special Government
messenger, he was passed on shore at
once without undergoing the ordeal of the
custom-house, and so contrived to catch the
special train that conveyed the mails to
London. He had by no means elaborated his
plan of action, and was anxiously revolving
in his mind how he should break the news to
Florence, when, upon the train drawing up at
Basingstoke, his eye was caught by something
in the advertisement of a weekly " society "
paper. The train ran past the spot ; but the
moment it stopped, Gerard jumped out, and
hurried back to look at the placard that had

attracted his attention. He had not been
mistaken; between the headings of " Naughty
Stories, No. 65," and an article on " Political
Masters," there appeared in large type, " The
Narborough Romance ! "

Gerard's first feeling was one of disappointment,
not unmixed with anger. He had
looked upon the fact of his being the only
possessor of the secret, with the exception of
those still on the spot, as an important point
in his favour in his further relations with
Florence, and as likely to form a bond of
union between them that must materially
advance his cause ; and now she would have
heard it all, and he would appear in a merely
secondary character. " That old priest has
gone and sent the telegram after all," he
thought ; and he execrated him in his heart
for not having kept his promise. The paper
was nearly a week old, and there was not a
copy left at the bookstall. Gerard hurriedly
questioned the man in charge, but he could
gather nothing from him, except that about
ten days before the papers had been full of
the case, and that he fancied it was a sort of

Ticliborne affair over again. But the man was
busy serving customers, and could give him
no attention ; and the train starting again
immediately, Gerard was obliged to resume
his journey without being any wiser, and
altogether in a most unsatisfactory frame of
mind.

Before arriving in London, however, he had
time to collect his thoughts ; and after some
reflection he decided that the best thing he
could do would be to go straight to his club,
where he could look up the papers, and see
what really had transpired. It was still early
in the day, and there would be no one at the
General Enquiry Office for another hour at
least. On arriving in London, therefore, he
drove, charter, luggage, and all, to the St
George's Club, where he was received by the
hall porter as if he had been there the day
before. Scarcely glancing at a budget of
letters that were handed to him, he made his
way to the morning room, at that time almost
empty, and pounced upon the last week's
Scandal.

With what trembling excitement Gerard

turned to and devoured the article in question
may readily be imagined ; but the more he
read, the more puzzled he became. It was
evident that the identity of Mr Montenello
with the former Lord Narborough had been
established, or, at any rate, suggested; but
no reference was made to the fact of his being
dead. On the contrary, much stress was
laid upon the awkward position likely to
be occupied by the present peer ; and speculations
were indulged in as to whether, supposing
the rightful heir chose to remain abroad
and relinquish all claims to the family possessions,
they could legally be enjoyed by
his nephew. " The position," continued the
writer, 'would have presented complications
of a still more remarkable nature if Lord
Narborough, as we must now call the eccentric
recluse of Venezuela, had any heirs male.
But we understand that the only issue of
the marriage has been a girl, who has
been brought up under an assumed name,
and under the charge of the Mr Baldwin
who has figured so prominently in this
strange story; and we may presume that.

whatever else may be decided upon, steps
will be forthwith taken by the Marlborough
family to have her position duly
recognised."

It was perfectly clear, therefore, that the
writer knew nothing of Mr Montenello's
death; and this made the whole thing quite
incomprehensible. " They can't have heard
anything from old Domenico, after all," he
thought ; " but what the devil can old Baldwin
have been doing ? " There was no time, however,
at the moment, for pursuing the subject
further ; for his first duty was to deliver the
precious charter, the object and cause of the
mission that had ended in such a strange and
unlooked-for adventure. So, reluctantly relinquishing
the thumbed and club worn copy
of Scandal, he re-entered his cab, which he
had left at the door, and drove off to the
General Enquiry Office.

Here he was greeted with much consideration
and respect by the messengers, as one
who had successfully braved the perils of the
deep in the interests of the office, and welcomed
almost exclusively by the great Mr

Mills. For once that eminent public servant
was quite overcome, and tears actually
stood in his eyes as he grasped the precious
document, the thought of which had
occupied his sleeping and waking hours for
the last two months or more. " You have
done very well, Courtenay," he said, " and
some day you will be glad to think that
you have been employed in this matter.
Some people think nothing of an affair of
this sort," he continued, calling to mind the
shocking levity displayed by the Vice-president
when the charter was first found to
be missing, " but you may depend upon it
that in these days one can't be too careful ;
and there is no saying what might not have
happened if we could not have produced the
charter at the proper time. However, all's
well that ends well, and the question won't
come before the House for another three
weeks, they say ; so we're all safe now.
Your chief will be here this afternoon, and
will be glad to see sou, no doubt. Goodbye,
then, for the present ; you see I am
rather busy just now."

So Gerard, feeling a certain sense of relief
in having got rid of his charge, repaired
to his own room, where he found the office-keeper
lighting the fire, and generally fussing
about. " Didn't know when to expect
you, sir," he said, " or I would have had
your room ready. His lordship has been
up for nearly a week, sir; will be glad to
have you back again, I should think, sir.
He said he should be here about three
o'clock, or perhaps a little earlier."

Acting on this information, Gerard resolved
to await his chiefs arrival before doing anything
further. He had from the first made
up his mind to confide entirely in him,
feeling that he could rely upon his sympathy
and counsel. He would, moreover, be certain
to have heard all about the affair, and
would be able to tell him in a few words
exactly what had happened. So he ordered
some luncheon, and at the same time requested
his friend the office-keeper to bring him the
files of the Times for the last fortnight. In
the meantime he proceeded to open a pile
of letters that had accumulated on his table.

and to generally resume occupation of this
room.

It is a curious feeling, that of coming back
to the quiet avocations of everyday life after
some unusually exciting interlude, especially
if the time, or a portion of it, has been spent
in a foreign country. After the first few
moments, everything seems just as if we had
never been away, and half an hour generally
suffices for us to drop insensibly into our long-accustomed
habits. There is something peculiarly
impressive, almost amounting to solemnity,
about the aspect of a room that has been
shut up during our absence ; and the mute
appearance of welcome about even inanimate
objects has for certain temperaments a peculiar
charm, suggesting the well-worn reflection
that after all there is no place like home.
Gerard, as he looked round his official room,
could hardly believe it possible that since
he was last there he had been on a voyage
to the tropics, and had passed through such a
strange experience. The housemaids of the
General Enquiry Office were not given to any
superfluous " tidying," and it certainly could

not be charged against them that they were
in the habit of unnecessarily disturbing books
and papers. Everything was just as he had
left it ; here a pile of blue-books that he had
been too lazy to clear away, there some old
official correspondence that he had got up for
some question in the House, and had put on
one side, thinking it might be required again ;
a book of travels in the West Indies, which he
had sent for from the office library to see what
sort of a place San Fernando was, still lay on
his table ; and in the blotting-book was a
sheet of paper with some rough memoranda
concerning his expedition. "By Jove," he
thought, "what strange things have happened
since I was here last ! " And he was obliged to
again have recourse to his precious letter, to
satisfy himself that even now it was not alla
dream.

Unfortunately, Gerard's intention to examine
the newspapers in order to get at the
truth of the Narborough story was for the
moment frustrated ; for his room was speedily
invaded by one member of the office after
another, some curious to hear his adventures

in connection with the recovered charter, and
others with papers, etc., for his chief. It was
a question upon which he naturally felt shy

and, at the time, there happened to be no

Under such circumstances Gerard was bound

Why, Gerard, my boy ! " he exclaimed, " I

Gerard recounted his general proceedings

Marlborough are friends of yours, are they

"Yes, I have heard something about it,"

Ah, well," returned Lord Eavendale, " it's
an extraordinary story. It seems that some
old fellow has died suddenly down in Weald-shire,

papers, they found a whole lot of documents,

VOL. III. I

from which it appears that the uncle of the
present Narborough, who was supposed to

A sudden light broke in upon Gerard. The
old priest had not broken his word after all,

If you don't mind," he said, "I should like

"My dear boy," replied his chief, taking

of course you know I'd always do anything

No ; it's not that, exactly," replied Gerard,

Lord Eavendale listened with the most undisguised

Yes," replied Gerard, sadly, as his mind

Lord Eavendale took the letter in his

Don't think about the office ; for the matter

All right then," checking Gerard's attempted

got to ask me."

So, about half-past seven the same evening,
Gerard found himself once more on
the platform at Shawfield station. He had
allowed himself a couple of hours, after

from whom he had more than once
received a tip during the previous winter.

Not been down here this year, sir ? " lie

Swan ? "

Yes," replied Gerard. " That's to say, I
want my things taken up there ; but, first

Netherdean, sir ? " repeated the man,

get one from the Station Hotel. I suppose

how do you mean ? "

Why, you see, sir, after the old gentleman

The deuce it is ! " muttered Gerard, in

'Bout a fortnight ago, I think, sir. Let's

Do you know where they went to ? " inquired

In a couple of minutes the porter was back,

Care of Mrs Ashton,
Scratby Hall

SkipivortJi Station,

North-Eastern Railway"

Gerard was not long in making up his mind.

'' When is there a train back to London ? '' he

"Nothing till 9.30, sir. There was one a

Very well, then," said Gerard, " I'll go

It was a comfort to have seen even her

Well, after aU, he was in a better position

under a lamp-post, to make sure that
even now it was not all a dream.

Scratch HALL.

Gerard had a slow and tedious journey back
to London, arriving at Victoria shortly
before midnight. He had procured a Bradshaw
at Shawfield, and had ascertained that
Skipworth was a little station on a branch
line starting from Newcastle, and that* by
leaving King's Cross by the " Flying Scotchman"
at ten in the morning, he could be
there about six o'clock in the evening. He
had decided not to return to his lodo:in2:s,
where he would not be expected, and accordingly
engaged a room at the Grosvenor Hotel.
He was up early next morning, fearful of missing
the train ; and after the usual rapid and
easy journey by what is unquestionably the
finest train service in the world, found himself
at Newcastle at four o'clock in the afternoon.
He had still nearly two hours' journey

before him, in a slow, stopping train, with
half an-hour's wait at another junction; but
he now felt that he really was drawing near
to Florence ; and in fact, when there remained
only two or three little wayside stations

strange sort of nervousness, and a sense of

after all, there should have been a mistake,
and she should have never seriously thought
about him at all ; or if, as was only too
probable during all this time, she had met

if indeed he had ever had any place there ?
His much-relied-on letter and all that had
passed would then be of no avail, and he
would only have the melancholy satisfaction
of as it were helping to clear the way for a
rival. So, as one queer-named station after
another was reached and passed, he became
more and more nervous and uncomfortable,
until, when at last the train drew up at the
bleak little platform, and a stentorian-voiced

accent, that this was no other than Skip-worth,
he almost wished himself anywhere
else. However, there he was, and he must
go through with it now. So, having given
up his ticket, and been well stared at as a
stranger, he inquired of the stationmaster
how far it was to Scratby Hall, and what
would be his best means of getting there.

Scratby Hall, it appeared, was six miles off,
up towards the hills. There was no available
vehicle at hand ; " but," added the stationmaster,
" you can get a machine at the
CoUingwood Arms, a mile up the road; and
here's Mr Copeland," addressing a jolly-looking
farmer who had been the only other
passenger for Skip worth, "has got his dogcart
here, and I daresay he'll give you a
lift as far as that, as it's all on his road
home."

The individual thus indicated expressed his
willingness to accommodate the gentleman, and
in another minute Gerard was seated by his
side and jogging along the road to the Col-logwood
Arms. Mr Copeland was not much
of a conversationalist ; or rather, he was perfectly

capable of expressing himself volubly
in bis own dialect, but as this was almost unintelligible
to Gerard, he could only answer
somewhat at random, and the conversation had
a tendency, therefore, to be rather one-sided.
Gerard essayed a question as to Scratby Hall
and its inmates, which elicited a torrent of

interesting, but which was almost entirely lost
upon our hero. He was just able to gather,
however, that Mrs Ashton was a lady of
whom Mr Copeland entirely approved, and
that Scratby Hall was " a bonny place."
They were soon at the CoUingwood Arms,
an old-fashioned posting-house standing by

incumbent upon Mr Copeland 'to alight and
have a glass of whisky, in which he insisted

hearty squeeze of the hand, he reascended his
dog-cart and went on his way.

In another ten minutes Gerard was on the
road again, this time in a four-wheeled

miles, as the driver was compelled to admit,
between himself and Scratby Hall. The
moon had now risen, and disclosed a pretty,
broken country, the low ground thickly

chances seemed in his favour, and it was

must necessarily be a sort of link between
them to which no one else could aspire.

His new charioteer was a quiet, civil-spoken
man, whose dialect was far more intelligible

shrewd, observant sort of fellow, as is often
the case with men of his calling ; and Gerard
was surprised to find that he not only knew

but was well acquainted with the fact that
a young lady was staying at the Hall who
had been recently discovered to be a daughter

of the Earl of Marlborough who was supposed
to have been killed many years ago. " It's
just a verra strange story," he observed, with
a flourish of his whip, " to think that a
nobleman like him should go and leave his
estates, and his family, and such like, just
to work like a mechanic. Arm thinking, too,
that it's no just right for a man, when he's
born into that state o' life, for to go and
leave all his duties behind him, just for a
mere fancy like. Weel, it's no for the
likes o' me to be finding fault with him ; and
maybe he'll come home now, when he hears
it's all found out, though arm thinking it
will be verra awkward for the young Earl
gin he does."

Gerard almost shuddered at the man's

A summons from the driver brought a

Gerard got down and rang the bell. Somehow

! Don't take my bag down," he said

Footsteps were heard approaching inside.

On some important business," he explained ;
I have just come from London on purpose."

The old man looked from Gerard to the

** If you will wait here a moment," continued

It is curious how, when our senses are
most highly strung, and what at the time
we may perhaps consider our life's happiness
may be trembling in the balance, our attention
will often be distracted by the most trivial

Gerard sat still for a few minutes, listening to

Gerard's embarrassment was not lost upon

Gerard hesitated. He did not quite like

Good gracious ! " exclaimed Mrs Ashton,

No, it was a mere accident," replied

Gerard followed his hostess along a passage

Gerard never knew how long lie was left
alone in that little room. It could not have

She had opened the door so noiselessly

For the moment Gerard could not trust

It's so kind of you to let me see you," heat
length replied, in a husky voice. " I went

''No," replied Gerard; "I have only been

From Venezuela ! " she exclaimed, with a

Gerard felt that the moment had come.

and the sooner it was over now the better.

Lady Florence," he said, taking her hand

She grew deadly pale, but did not withdraw

I have seen your father, and have heard

darling, I have a sad story to tell you, and
and you will never see him again in this
world." And he fairly broke down himself.


For a few moments Gerard hardly knew
what was going on. The room seemed to

swim round with him, and he was only dimly
conscious of a sweet, pale face looking at
him with a scared and dazed expression, and
of a feeling that, somehow or other, he himself
was to blame for it all. She had sunk
down on a sofa, and sat with her hands folded,
gazing straight before her with a look of
blank despair. It was a fixed intensity of
grief that was most distressing to look upon ;
and Gerard, overpowered by the love which
he could no longer conceal, and distracted

indirectly been the means of bringing upon
her, could hardly contain himself.

Florence, dearest Florence," he said, kneeling
down by her side, and striving to possess
himself again of her hand, "it is dreadful
for me to think that I should have had
to bring you this terrible news, and I little
thought what was to come when I left 'Eiicr-
land two months ago. But there has been
some special providence in it, Florence, I do
believe," he said to himself. " I can't tell
you all now ; it would be too much for
you. But let me give you this, which he

charged me to deliver into your own hands."
And he drew out the precious letter.

She turned her head, and looked at him
with a sad, wistful expression in those sweet
hazel eyes, and took the letter from him
almost mechanically, gazing at the address as
if she hardly comprehended it. But she still
neither spoke nor shed tears, and Gerard
began to be seriously alarmed.

Fortunately, at this moment a diversion was
effected by the entrance of Mrs Ashton, who,
thinking that she had left the young people
together quite long enough, and perhaps Idiot
without a certain feminine curiosity to learn
what Gerard's visit could be about, rustled
in with some empressement. " I am sorry to
disturb you," she began, ^' but it's nearly a
quarter to eight, and " But her observations
were cut short by catching sight of
Florence's pale and scared face, and of Gerard's
anxious and distressed expression, which indicated
unmistakably that something unusual
had taken place.

"Dear me," she exclaimed, "what is the
matter? Florence, my dear, what has happened

? Mr Courtenay, what have you been
saying to her ? "

Gerard cast one look at Florence, and then
stepped forward. "Mrs Ashton," he said,
I have had a very serious communication to
make to Lady Florence, as I told you, and it
has upset her very much, as I feared it would.
I think I had better leave her now, and it will
be best for her to be with you alone. I will
wait till you send for me, and then I will tell
you all about it, if you wish it." And he
moved towards the door.

There was something in his manner that
impressed Mrs Ashton, and made her feel
that he was in the right. "Yes, go," she
said, hurriedly. " Florence, my child," advancing
towards her with outstretched arms, " tell
me what it is, and perhaps I may be able to
help you."

Gerard did not dare look behind him, but
went quickly out of the room. In the hall
he found his friend Mr Gregson hanging
about in a state of the most intense curiosity,
and evidently engaged in imparting his views
to a footman, who listened to him with the

respect due to a superior officer who was
to a certain extent in the secrets of the
family.

"Look here," said Gerard, addressing the
ancient retainer, "I wish to wait here a little,
as I think Mrs Ashton will wish to see me
again by and by. Just show me into some
room where I can wait, and then come and
let me know whenever she wishes to seeme."


He spoke with an air of decision and
authority, and the old man seemed to divine
instinctively that something unusual had
happened. He bowed gravely, and conducted
Gerard to a little room opening out of the
other end of the hall, a sort of little study
or business room, lighted only by the occasional
flickering of a fire that was smouldering
in an old-fashioned grate. " TU get a
light in a moment, sir," he said, bustling
about and stirring up the fire; "and won't
you take a glass of wine or something, sir?
Dinner was put off" for half an hour, and it's
long past that now."

Gerard declined the osier of refreshment,

VOL. III. L

and having with some difficulty persuaded
his would-be entertainer to leave him alone
in the dark, he threw himself into an armchair
before the fire, and leant his burning
forehead on his hand. It was a relief to him to
be alone, and to stare vacantly into the glowing
coals, wondering vaguely how Florence was
getting on, and what was to happen next.
It was impossible for him to collect his ideas
sufficiently to reflect calmly on what had
passed; but through the whirl of conflicting

to Florence the sacred missive from her
father's deathbed ; and above all, there was a

that she could hardly have received him
in th way she had done unless he had at
least some place in her heart.

Worn out by excitement, following on a succession
of broken nights and a long journey,
a sense of drowsiness gradually overcame

ofi* into a sort of feverish slumber, occasionally
awakening with a start as a coal dropped out

eliot up from the smouldering embers, when

"Dear me, Mr Courtenay, you are all in

poor Florence "

"Never mind a light, Mrs Ashton," exclaimed

"Ah, poor child," replied Mrs Ashton,
she is terribly upset, and no wonder. Dear

What ! shot by Indians, and died the same

And the good lady would have run on in

But tell me, I entreat you," he repeated,
do you think it has affected her very much ?
I feel as if I were responsible for it all, somehow,

"No, poor dear, you can't see her now;
we have taken her up to her room, and I

and she has had a good cry, which has
done her good ; but of course it is a great

nearly nine. You will stop here all night,
of course, and I will tell them to have dinner

Gerard could not refuse such an offer.
You are very kind indeed," he said. " I had

thought of stopping at the Collingwood Arms,
but perhaps"

Oh, dear no," exclaimed Mrs Ashton. " I
couldn't think of such a thing. No ; of course
you must stop here. Ill go and see about
your room at once, and then you must have
some dinner." And she bustled out of the

Gerard remained there for a moment, and
then, remembering that all this time his dogcart
was presumably waiting at the door, he
made his way through the hall, and found the

to circumstances, fast asleep on the doorstep,
while the horse, with a rug thrown over
him, appeared to have followed his example.
Having roused his charioteer, and liberally
compensated him ; and having ascertained,
moreover, that his services could be obtained,
if desired, on the following day, he returned
into the hall, and awaited the course of
events.

He had not much longer to wait before the
old butler reappeared, and informed him that
his room was all ready, and that dinner had

been ordered for him in a quarter of an hour.
'' Mrs Ashton desired me to give you her
compliments, sir," continued the old gentleman,
in a confidential tone, "and to say that
she was sure you'd excuse her, but she has
gone back to Lady Florence, and she didn't
like to leave her just now."

Gerard found a snug little bedroom assigned
to him, which, although hastily prepared,
seemed to him the picture of comfort. The fire
was already burning brightly, casting a warm
glow over the prettily-papered walls ; a charming
little French bed suggested pleasant ideas
of repose ; and the thought that he was for the
first time to pass the night under the same
roof with Florence was, despite his anxiety, a
very delightful one.

Under the circumstances we have narrated,
an orthodox and traditional hero would of
course have had very little appetite for his
dinner. He would have pushed away his
plate untasted, and have just touched a glass
of wine with his lips, setting it down again
with a heavy sigh. Or possibly he might
have sought relief in intoxication, and have

swallowed bumper after bumper with the
feverish recklessness denoting a mind ill at
-ease. In any case his repast would have
been an eminently unsatisfactory one. We
regret to state that none of these conditions
were fulfilled by our hero. On the contrary,
whether from fatigue or excitement, he found
himself to be decidedly hungry, and did ample
justice to a very nice little dinner and two
or three glasses of excellent claret. He could
certainly have dispensed with the officious
attentions of Mr Gregson, who having begun
by regarding him with something approaching
to suspicion, had rapidly come round to \h.e
conclusion that he was a person to be encouraged
and made much of But having at
last got rid of him, he drew his chair up to
the fire, feeling aU .the better for his dinner,
and prepared for any emergency.

He was wondering how Florence was feeling,
and thinking what he ought to do next,
when old Gregson returned, and informed
him that Mrs Ashton would be glad to see
him before he went to bed. Starting up,
he followed the old man to the little morning

room where he had met Florence, and found
his hostess alone.

I hope they have made you comfortable, •
Mr Courtenay," she said. " I am so sorry to
have left you alone, but I didn't like to leave
poor Florence, you know. But she has gone
to bed now, poor child, and I hope she will
go to sleep by and by."

'' How is she now, Mrs Ashton ? " asked
Gerard, anxiously. " Do you think I shall
be able to see her in the morning ? "

Oh well, I hope so," she returned, looking
curiously and not unkindly at him. It is
hardly necessary to say that, with a woman's

Gerard had a deeper interest in Florence than ,
was to be accounted for by the mere fact of

her father. Gerard saw that she had guessed
his secret, and longed to sound her as to
Florence's feelings.

But whether from prudence or reserve,
Mrs Ashton did not appear desirous of encouraging
his confidence. "Poor child," she
continued, "it is very hard for her, is it

not, when she has never known her father
all her life, to have him taken from her just
as it had all come out, you know ? I'm sure
I don't know what she will do. Only fancy,
though, if he had lived, what an awkward

best, who can tell ? But we must talk about
all this tomorrow, Mr Courtenay. I am
sure you must be very tired, and I shall wish
you good-night."

And with sundry hopes that he would find
himself comfortable, and sleep well, the good
lady retired. There was nothing then left
for Gerard but to go to bed himself.

all's well that ends well.

When Gerard awoke the next morning, it
was some moments before he was able to
realise where he was. Three weeks at sea
are apt to somewhat derange and unsettle
one's home ideas ; and Gerard, who, except
for the one night at the Grosvenor Hotel,
had passed that time in a steamboat berth,
was for the moment quite at a loss to account
for his present situation. ' First he thought
he was at Sydmonton, and then at San Fernando,
and then, as the train of his thoughts
led him to Venezuela and Florence, the real
state of things suddenly flashed upon him.

He looked at his watch, and found that it
was a quarter to eight. Daylight was showing
itself through the chinks of his shutters, and
making the objects in the room just visible
in a dim and uncertain sort of way. He

jumped out of bed and proceeded to open
the window. It was a soft February morning
; the mist was lifting here and therefrom
the hollows in the park ; the first
rays of the sun were lighting up the dark
fir woods and the distant hills ; the rooks
were already astir, winging their way in
noisy battalions to their feeding - grounds,
and everything looked bright and cheerful.
What a grand day for a hunt ! " was
Gerard's first thought ; and the, leaving
the window open, he betook himself again
to bed, to think over the situation at
leisure.

Somehow or other, and despite the anxiety
he could not help feeling, the aspect of things
in general seemed much brighter to him this
morning. It was an incalculable relief to him
to feel that he had fulfilled his sacred duty
of breaking the news to Florence, and delivering
to her the letter from her father. And
he could not disguise from himself that to
whatever extent she might for the time being
be affected by the intelligence of her father's
death, it could not possibly be the same

thing as if she had known him as a father all
her life ; and that however poignant might
be her distress and disappointment at being
deprived of a father at the very moment when
he had been revealed to her, there could not
be the same feeling of lasting regret in the
ease of a parent whom she had hitherto regarded
as nothing more than a friend, and
whom, however warm that friendship might
have been, she had only been in the habit of
seeing at rare and intermittent intervals.

While meditating deeply over his prospects,
he heard a knock at his door, and his friend
Mr Gregson entered with his clothes and hot
water. Having made the necessary arrangements

the bed, and informed him in a confidential
manner that it was eight o'clock. "Mrs
Ashton," he added, "wished me to give you
her kind regards, sir, and to tell you that
Lady Florence has had some sleep, and seems
better this morning."

Gerard expressed his gratification in a
befitting manner, and then, when the old man
had left the room, buried his face in the

pillow with a thrill of delight. For, apart
from the relief which he naturally felt at
hearing such comparatively good news of
Florence, the mere fact of it being considered
necessary to send him a message about her
seemed to him to have a most delightful
significance. It looked like a recognition of
his interest in her — a sort of concession of
proprietorship, in fact. And could it be possible
that she had sent him the message
herself, or suggested that it should be
sent to him ? No, this could hardly be ;
but it was very delightful to be thus associated
with her. So he got up, and performed
his toilet in a decidedly hopeful
frame of mind ; and then, at the summons
of a gong, went downstairs with a beating
heart.

He was met in the hall by old Gregson,
who conducted him to the dining-room, where
a few moments later he was joined by his
hostess. " I hope you slept well, Mr Courtenay,"
she said, after the usual greetings,
"and found everything comfortable in your
room. I sent you a message about Florence,

as I was sure you would be anxious to hear
how she was. Poor child, she was in a dreadful
state last night ; but she got to sleep at
last, and slept for three or four hours. I
took her up some tea myself about an
hour ago, and she has gone to sleep again,
so I hope she will be much better when
she wakes."

Gerard murmured something about Mrs
Ashton's kindness, and his own distress at
having been the bearer of such sad news.
"It must have seemed so extraordinary to

I was bound to find her out at once, and give

I may perhaps have broken it too suddenly

Oh, no," replied Mrs Ashton, " I am sure
you did quite right. When these things have

so close on my poor brother's death too.

You see, Mr Courtenay," lie continued,
speaking in such a feeling and natural manner

all her life. She has always looked upon

this yet ; — poor young man, he wrote Florence

"Yes," replied Gerard, not without a certain

; but— but I ought to see Florence — Lady

Oh, well, I daresay you might," replied

But, do you know, Mr Courtenay, I can't help

apart from this sad affair "

Mrs Ashton," broke in Gerard, actuated

about the bush, and I may as well tell you

and I ventured to tell her so more than a

her, or I can't bear it any longer. Of course
I couldn't say anything yesterday, you know ;
but don't you think I might see her again
before I go this evening ? "

It is an old saying that all women are by
nature matchmakers at heart, and that their
instincts will generally be on the side of a
true love affair — often all the more for its
being utterly undesirable or unreasonable.
Mrs Ashton's disposition was certainly not
such as to make her an exception to this rule.
She had guessed how matters stood from the

was with intelligence of such strange and

momentous import, was all in his favour ;

I thought there was something of this

o o

sort, Mr Courtenay," she said, with a kind

Well, I think you look as if you might

sure I will not stand in your way. Now

Two o'clock found our hero hanging about
in a state of nervous anxiety and impatience.
With the praiseworthy object of distracting
his mind, and giving himself something todo,
he had started off on a walk which he
calculated would not bring him back till
luncheon time. He had found his way upto
the keeper's house, and having made
friends with that functionary, inspected the
kennel, and listened, with as much appearance

of interest as he could command, to a retrospective
sketch of the past shooting season
in that district ; had struck away across the
park in the direction of the hills, and, effecting
a detour of some seven or eight miles, had
got back to the house at about half-past one.
Under any other circumstances he would
have enjoyed his walk immensely that bright
February morning ; and even as it was, the
fresh air and exercise did him good, and to
a certain extent brought about the object
he had in view. It is needless to say, however,
that the one absorbing thought of
Florence was ever present to him, and he
found himself perpetually looking at his
watch, and counting the half-hours that
seemed to pass so slowly. And once having
got into difficulties by attempting a shortcut
through a wood, which only led him,
after much wandering about, into a hopeless
sort of ravine, from which he was compelled,
with much trouble, to retrace his
steps, he got into a perfect fever of apprehension
lest he should get back late, and so
miss the chance of seeing her.

He found his way back, however, by half-past
one, and having been informed that
luncheon would be ready at two, proceeded
to establish himself in the hall, and go
through the form of reading the papers,
casting an impatient glance at intervals to
the slow-moving hands of the clock. It
was evident that punctuality was a leading
feature in the internal economy of Mrs
Ashton's household, for at two o'clock precisely
the gong sounded, and a few moments
later the rustle of a dress was heard along;
the corridor, the door was thrown open by
Mr Gregson, who had been lying in wait
behind it, and the lady of the house appeared
once more, but, alas ! alone.

"I hope you have had a nice walk, Mr
Courtenay," she said ; " I am sure you will
be glad of some luncheon. I am afraid we
shall be quite alone," leading the way into
the dining-room; "but Florence will not be
down just now. She is better, though, and
has had some more sleep this morning, and
I think she will perhaps be down by and
by."

Gerard followed his hostess into the diningroom
with a feeling of vexation and disappointment,
for although he had hardly
hoped to see Florence at luncheon, he had
indulged in the hope that she would perhaps
send him a message, or in some way, at any
rate, recognise his being there. But he felt
all the more determined to try and see her
again and bring matters to an issue before
he left that evening. With this object in
view, he broached the subject of his departure
as soon as the servants had left the
room. "I see there is a train from Skip-worth
at seven, Mrs Ashton," he remarked
in a tone of assumed indifference, "which
will catch the mail at Newcastle ; and if
you will allow me, I will order a .trap
from the Collingwood Arms to be here at
six. The man who brought me here yesterday
said that a message by the postman
from here would reach them by eve o'clock,
so that will be plenty of time."

Dear me ! must you really go, Mr Courtenay
? " exclaimed Mrs Ashton. " I do wish
you could stay with us for a day or two ; it

seems to me that there is so much to talk
about, and so many things I should like to
ask you ; but it has all been such a shock tome
that I can hardly think of anything at
present. And then, Florence, you know— of
course you must see her ; but I really don't
know whether she will be up to it this
afternoon."

But Gerard had grown almost desperate.

Mrs Ashton," he said, " I really must go
to-night, for you know this ought to be told
to Narborough at once. But I must see her
before I go. If all goes well — you know what
I mean — I shall hope to come back here again
very soon ; that's to say, if you will let me.
But see her I must ; and I am sure you will
not stand in the way, will you ? "

He spoke almost beseechingly, and yet in
such a tone as showed Mrs Ashton that he
was in earnest.

Yes, I believe you are right," she replied,
after a moment's thought, "and she had
better see you. Wait here a few minutes,
Mr Courtenay, and I will go and speak to
her now."

Gerard had not long to wait. In ten
minutes Mrs Ashton had returned, with the
intelligence that Florence would be down in
half an hour. '' And she will see you in my
room," continued the good lady, with a benevolent
smile. " Now, you had better go and
take a turn on the terrace, and then you can
come in and wait for her."

If any one had looked into Mrs Ashton's
little sitting-room about an hour and a half
later, they might have seen two persons
sitting on a sofa in close proximity to each
other, and apparently on the best possible
terms. They do not talk much, but they
appear to be very happy, notwithstanding.
If either of them were asked, they would
probably be unable to say exactly how it was
that they found themselves in that position,
but now it seems to come very natural. She
has a letter in one hand, and the other is held
captive by her companion, who shows no inclination
to let it go. Suddenly she lifts her
head, and looks in his face, with a half-shy,
half-amused smile.

*^ What is it, my darling ? " lie says, and his
arm steals round her slender waist, as he draws
her still nearer to him.

Gerard," she whispers, with a lovely blush,
as she hides her head on his shoulder, " do you
know, I can't help thinking that I must have
loved you all the time ! "

FAMILY AFFAIRS.

We have devoted so much attention to following
up the fortunes of our hero that we fear we
have somewhat neglected the other characters
of our story, to whom, therefore, we hasten to
return, with every apology for having kept
them so long standing on one side. And
having resuscitated a gentleman who had
been supposed to be dead for many years, a
circumstance that could hardly be otherwise
than disquieting and embarrassing to his
family circle, our first duty is clearly towards
those likely to be most directly affected
by such an event.

It is hardly necessary to observe that the
discovery of the existence of a previous Lord
Narborough, brought to light by certain
documents found among Mr Baldwin's papers
at that gentleman's decease, had excited considerable

interest and speculation, not only
among the members of the Middleton family,
but in society at large. The effect produced
by the intelligence upon the present holder
of the title was, if somewhat peculiar,
at least such as might be said to do
credit to his feelings. He forthwith betook
himself to bed, from which retreat he
despatched a summons to his solicitor, requesting
his immediate attendance. Upon
the arrival of the man of law, he found his
noble client, with a pale but determined face, in
the act of drawing up the draft of a document,
apparently intended to be something between
a will and an affidavit, whereby he desired to
renounce all claim to the Narborough title and
estates in favour of his resuscitated uncle.
I, Thomas Middleton," so ran this remarkable
production, "hitherto styled Earl
of Narborough, Baron Easthampton, &c., &c.,
being sound of body, though in great distress
of mind, do hereby, and in the presence
of witnesses ." Beyond this

point, however, his lordship had not as yet

could leave no room for doubt as to his entire
and voluntary relinquishment of title, estates,
and personal property. "My wife, as you are

'has now two hundred a year of her own; and
I daresay some of my friends will be able to
help me to some ap-appointment that will keep
us and our children from starving."

It was with some difficulty that the lawyer
succeeded in persuading his lordship that any
such action as he proposed must necessarily
be premature ; that, after all, no absolute proof
was as yet forthcoming as to the truth of the
extraordinary story that had been set on foot ;
that even if his uncle really were alive, as
stated, it rested with him to come forward
and assert his claim ; and that, until he did
so, the accepted holder of the title was unquestionably
its legal representative. It was
some time before this reasoning had any
effect; but his lordship at length consented

to postpone any action for the present, and
await the course of events. " Wait for a
couple of months, anyhow, my lord," pleaded
his legal adviser; "if your uncle is really
alive, which I must confess I very much
doubt, he will know by that time that his
secret has transpired, and he will be bound
to take some steps in the matter, or, at any
rate, to let us know what he means to do."

So it was settled that no outward alteration
should take place for the present in the status
or circumstances of the Narborough family,
and that everything should, as far as possible,
go on as usual. His lordship left his bedroom,

though rather with the air of a gentleman

Poor Lady Narborough had, as was only

Matters had been in this state for about
a fortnight, when one morning, about eleven

say that the next arrival might not be the
real original Lord Narborough himself, come

was therefore experienced when the
occupant of the vehicle was recognised by

What on earth can he want here just now ? "
thought the groom of the chambers to himself,

Her ladyship," muttered the footman to
his superior, as he passed Gerard on to his
hands.

'' Yes, Tompkins," said Gerard, with a nod

He felt that it would be easier and more

about Florence. So lie waited
patiently in the empty library till informed

He found Lady Narborough sitting at a

"Oh yes, I have heard all about it,"

In fact," he continued, with some hesitation,

Why, what can you mean, Gerard ? "

"Well, I may as well tell you at once'
said Gerard, feeling that it was best to

And without more ado he proceeded to

ended.

Lady Narborough listened with greatness

Oh, Gerard ! " she gasped out, " is this

Gerard shook his head, with a smile.

It's all as true as that I am sitting here,"
he replied, "though sometimes I can hardly
believe it myself; but there will be plenty

of others to prove it by and by, if necessary."

*eyes," replied Gerard, with a strange,

'well, Gerard," said Lady Narborough,

He must be told this at once," she continued,

This, however, was more than our friend

"Ob, Gerard, must you really go? But

You have plenty of time, you know ; it's not
one yet."

So, after a hasty luncheon, and a warm

Having no feelings of sentiment in this

He made a few careful notes of

"Then there's nothing more for me to doat

Oh, of course I shall see to that," replied

Lord Narborough's father, you know, and all

Ah ! " thought Gerard, as he was bowed

He had not thought it necessary to take

He found the Vice-president busy with a

Gerard readily assented, feeling that a

He seemed somewhat preoccupied, and

Eight o'clock saw our friend in Grosvenor

"His lordship is dressing, sir," observed

The Vice-president and his private secretary

He made him tell his whole story over

By Jove," he said, " I think you're in

and then I suppose there'll be some money,

I really don't know about that," replied

We must do our hero the justice to say

Ah, well," observed Lord Eavendale, as he

I have often wanted," said Gerard at

BLESS YOU, MY CHILDREN ! "

As Lord Eavendale had predicted, it was not

had thought it advisable to make further

in writing to announce his return.

that he had a communication of importance to

early until within a few months of the time

Yes, Jerry," she said — for she was beginning

night, I do believe. He had been quite well
up to the day before, but a change seemed to

we all went to bed as usual. He used often

next morning I was awoke by a great talking

They found him, Jerry," she went on,

My darling," replied Gerard, not without

that it might have happened at anytime
? "

Oh, yes," she returned, " and I'm sure

I am afraid I never thought much about it,

And to think that I shall never see his dear

Now, too, for the first time, Gerard became

But I can't remember anything whatever
