and stay some time."

It was inexpressibly sweet to Gerard to sit
by Florence's side and listen to her half shy,
half-eager confidences respecting her past life ;
and sweeter still was it to watch the daily
improvement that was apparent in her health
and spirits, to see the colour returning to her
cheek, and the scared, sorrowful look that

seemed to have taken possession of her
features gradually giving place to an expression
of rest and happiness.

She has had a terrible shock, poor child,"
said Mrs Ashton to Gerard, " but she certainly
seems to be getting over it wonderfully, and I
must say I don't think I have ever seen her
look so really hap23y before."

The crowning event of those happy days
was Gerard's journey to London in company
with Mrs Ashton and Florence. A renewed
and pressing invitation had come from Lady
Narborough on behalf both of herself and her
lord, now restored to a tolerable degree of
equanimity, expressing their desire to welcome
Florence into the family at as early a date as
possible. It was only fitting and natural that
this ceremony should not be unnecessarily
delayed, and it had been arranged, therefore,
that Florence was to meet and be introduced
to the Marlborough in London, and then
accompany them on a short visit to Easthampton.


An introduction to a new set of relations is

always a more or less trying and awkward
affair. " I felt so dreadfully shy at first,"
said Florence to Gerard afterwards, " and as if
I had no business there at all ; but they were
all so kind and nice, I soon felt at home with
them. Lady Narborough is charming, and he
was very kind too, though I couldn't help
feeling a little frightened of him. But I think
I shall like Lady Adelaide best of all, Jerry, —
she is quite charming, and such fun too, Fm
sure. She told me you and she were great
friends, Jerry, — oh yes, I quite understand ! "
as Gerard, half laughing, half blushing, endeavoured
to explain their quasi-relationship.
Yes, a very nice cousin to have indeed ; but
you needn't flatter yourself, sir, that I'm going
to be jealous of her ; and besides, she is going
to marry your chief, so you will have to mind
what you are about now. Isn't it funny,
Jerry, that you and he should be going to
marry two cousins ? and you and I are almost
a sort of cousins too, I suppose. Oh dear !
how shall I ever get to remember that my
name is Middleton and not Graham ! "

London was beginning to fill, and the affairs
of the Narborough family being still to a
certain extent before the public, the double
engagement of Lady Adelaide Middleton and
her newly-found cousin Lady Florence to a
well-known member of the Government and
his private secretary excited no small stir in
the fashionable world. Much curiosity was
manifested regarding Florence, and Lady Marlborough
was besieged with inquiries about the
new cousin and her romantic story. Public
opinion, we regret to state, was not in favour
of her engagement to Gerard ; and it was held
that he had as it were stolen a march upon
society, which had some reason to complain of
not being in a position to pass a verdict upon
this new scion of the Narborough family before
her affections had become engaged and her
future sealed.

Wonderful story, isn't it ? " observed Colonel
Scott to Mount-eaton, as he met him on
the steps of White's one afternoon. " Do you
remember our talking it over in the smoking-room
last year at Bellmoor, and your asking

me whether there had ever been any doubt
about his death ? By Jove ! it only shows what
a keen, long-headed fellow old Brentford must
have been. Why, it's just the very thing
he foresaw when he wouldn't agree with the
others, though of course every one laughed
at him then. Anything in the evening
papers ? "

Gerard's first reception by Lady Adelaide
after their respective engagements was extremely
characteristic of that young lady.
"Well, you're a nice young man, upon my
word," she began, "to go and discover our
new cousin in this sort of way, and then get
engaged to her before any of us have had time
to make her acquaintance I It's just about
the coolest thing I ever heard of. I told her
you wanted a lot of keeping in order, and that
she mustn't stand any nonsense. Me engaged
too ? Well, what business is that of yours ?
You'd better be careful how you speak to me
now, or I shall tell your chief. He says he
doesn't think much of you as a private secretary,
and could easily get a better one."

We are bound to confess, however, that they
parted on the most excellent terms. " I told
Florence you weren't a bad sort of boy on
the whole, Jerry," she said, " and I think myself
that you're uncommonly lucky," a sentiment
that our friend was quite prepared to
endorse.

AFTER LONG YEARS.

Our story is drawing to a close ; and having,
indeed, piloted our hero through a series of
modest adventures into the calm waters of
matrimony, or, at any rate, within an appreciable
distance of that highly honourable institution,
beyond which it is not our purpose
to follow him, we feel that we have no further
claim upon the indulgence of the reader, beyond
what is necessary to wind up the affairs of
our remaining characters with a due regard to
decency and good order.

It was only natural, having regard to the
exceptional circumstances under which Lady
Florence Middleton had been introduced to
society, that rumour should have credited her
with being a considerable heiress. It was
asserted by some that the late Lord Marlborough
had, as Mr Montenello, amassed a

large fortune by land speculations in Venezuela,
wMcli had been invested in English
securities for his daughter's benefit. Others
spoke with confidence of charges on the Marlborough
estates that would have to be paid,
with long arrears of interest, by the present
Earl ; while others whispered of sugar plantations
and shares in silver mines that she
would inherit from her mother. And all were
agreed in' the opinion that whatever property
had been left by Mr Baldwin would certainly
come to her.

These speculations were not, however, justified
by the result. When, after a time, the
position of the late Lord Narborough's affairs
had been thoroughly mastered by the lawyers,
it turned out that the property left by him
was not likely to realise any considerable
value. His will was found among Mr Baldwin's
papers, in which he bequeathed everything
to his daughter Florence. But as
regards the much-talked-of Venezuelan property,
it appeared that but little value was
attached to it even by the owner. In a
memorandum attached to the will, it was

stated that having, previously to his supposed
decease, transferred a certain sum of money
to America, he had embarked in some oil
speculations which had succeeded in a most
remarkable manner, and which had of themselves
placed him in a position of independence.
That having after a time decided
upon settling in Venezuela, he had invested
the greater part of his capital in the purchase
and stocking of the estate of Los Angels,
and in the erection from time to time of a
hacienda and other works and buildings ; but
that, on the whole, the enterprise had been
a disappointment, and that, after thirty years
spent in an endeavour to establish a settlement
on a thoroughly substantial basis, he
had been forced reluctantly to the conclusion
that the unsettled state of the country and
the unsatisfactory system of government
rendered it improbable that after his death it
would be carried on in the same manner with
a reasonable prospect of success. The value
of the land itself was little or nothing, and
the only property worth realising would bethe
hacienda itself, and the live-stock, plant.

and buildings, which would doubtless command
a certain price at the hands of any one
who should be inclined to take over the estate
as a commercial speculation. The testator
requested, therefore, that Andrew Baldwin,
Esq., of Netherdean, AVealdshire, and his
nephew, the Earl of Narborough, whom he
named as his daughter's trustees, should take
such steps as seemed good to them, after due
consultation with certain persons mentioned
as authorities on such a matter, either to
realise the available property at once, or to
lease the estate to any suitable party who
might be disposed to embark in it. A certain
sum was to be set apart from the proceeds
either of the lease or sale of the property,
which was to be divided in legacies among: a
number of persons on the estate, whose names
were specified in a schedule attached ; and
a more recent note was appended to the
memorandum, recommending to the special
care of the succeeding Earl of Narborouo:h
Joseph Diadems, formerly of Easthampton,
but then residing at Los Angelos, in the event
of his desiring to return to England. The

VOL. III. p

remainder of his property consisted, it appeared,
of ten thousand pounds invested in
various English securities, a special renunciation
being made of all claim upon any-personal
property accruing from the Nary
estate, which it was desired should
pass absolutely into the hands of his successor.


As regarded Mr Baldwin's property, it
appeared that the greater part of his income
had been derived from an annuity, and that
the remainder of his personal property had
been bequeathed to a nephew. The small
freehold of Netherdean was left, as has
already transpired, to Florence ; but as this
consisted merely of the house and about
thirty acres of woodland and meadow, it
could hardly be described as landed property
of any importance. Still, although
not absolutely an heiress according to modern
ideas. Lady Florence Middleton was unquestionably
the owner of a residence in Weald-shire,
ten thousand pounds in good investments,
and an estate in Venezuela of problematical
value. To her and Gerard, indeed,

being both in that blissful state when
everything appears couleur de rose, such
prospects appeared to open up a vista of
untold riches ; and the possession of Nether-dean
alone would have seemed to them to
afford a sufficient provision upon which to
commence their married life.

But perhaps the most deeply interesting to
Florence of the various documents that were now
brought to lio^ht was the record of the marriage
of Francis Middleton of Easthampton, Weald-shire,
England, to Isidora, daughter of Don
Manuel da Silva, of New York and Havana,
which was stated to have taken place at tie
British Consulate at the latter town in the
year 1853. The most complete evidence was
forthcoming as to the legality of the ceremony,
and little difficulty had been experienced in
satisfactorily identifying Francis Middleton
with Francis Earl of Narborough. In a few
touching lines it was recorded how they had
lived together at Los Angelos for six years,
when Florence's birth took place, followed,
within a few seeks, by the death of her
mother. She had been buried at Caracas,

where they had been staying at the time, and
where her husband desired that he himself
might be laid by her side.

It had been decided that Florence and
Gerard were to be married in July, by which
time six months would have elapsed since
Lord Narborough's death. In the meantime,
Florence was to remain with Mrs
Ashton, who had begged to be allowed to
keep her till the last ; and a move having
been made to Portland Place, Gerard was
placed in unrestricted communication with
his fiancee.

Oh, Jerry ! " she exclaimed, as he arrived
one evening from his office, " who do you
think I have seen to-day ? I told you about
Mrs Caledon, who was my governess, you
know. I have heard nothing of her for
years, but I met her in Bond Street to-day,
looking so handsome, and she has married
again. She is Mrs Morton now, and she
says that she thinks her husband knows you.
Can it possibly be the Mr Morton you have
told me so much about ? "

By Jove ! " exclaimed Gerard ; " no, it

surely can't be ; and yet, I don't know who
else it could be. He hasn't got any brother,
or any relation who could know me. No,
I do believe it must be old George! Yes,
I see it all now ; she must have been his
old love; he as good as told me he had
had some affair of that kind, and had never
got over it. But I can t make out why he
didn't tell me something about it. Well,
if it is him, it's a real good business,
and I'm most awfully glad. But I
must try and find out at once. She
didn't tell you where they were staying I
suppose ? "

No, I quite forgot to ask her ; how
stupid of me ! But I shall see her again
soon, for she is coming here to tea on
Thursday, and is to bring her husband ; so
you must be sure to come and meet her."

"All right," replied Gerard; 'but I'll
find out before that. They are sure to know
at his old lodgings, and I'll go down there
tomorrow. By Jove ! now I think of it, the
last time I was there, his landlady seemed

to think there was something up, but I
never thought about it then."

It must not be supposed that our hero
had all this time been altogether unmindful
of his old friend. But he had not heard
from Morton since his return from abroad,
although he had written to him at his address
at Starlington, and had called once or
twice in Hatfield Street. He had, however,
been unable to obtain any information
there beyond the fact that Mr Morton had
allowed his landlady to sub - let his rooms
for six months from Christmas. Knowing
his friend's peculiar disposition and habits,
he had thought it best to leave him alone,
feeling sure that he would hear of him
again as soon as his affairs altered for the
better.

''My darling, you were quite right," was
his greeting to Florence as he appeared in
Portland Place the following evening; *rit's
quite true, and the old lady is in a great
state of excitement, *been there with the
lady, and introduced her, — so handsome, so
nice and all that sort of thing ; but she is

evidently rather divided in her mind between
her admiration of Mrs George and her regret
at losing such a good lodger. Well, I am
glad it's all right with him at last, poor old
boy; and only fancy her having been at
Netherdean with you, too ! How curiously
things come round I Why, it was odd enough
that old Martin should have lived with you,
but this beats everything."

"I say, George, old fellow," said Gerard,
as they got alone together, after the little
reunion in Portland Place on the following
Thursday, " you might have let me know
about this ; you must have thought me an
awful brute not to take any notice of it, but
how the deuce was I to know ? "

"My dear boy," replied Morton, "I did
write to you — about Christmas it was — but
perhaps you didn't get the letter, as that must
have been about the time you were in Venezuela."


"Ah, that's it, then," returned Gerard,
reflectively. " I have no doubt it was forwarded
to me, and is perhaps knocking about
San Fernando now. I fancied I had missed

one or two letters about that time. Perhaps
it will turn up yet."

And, sure enough, the letter did turn up,
a fortnight later, together with half-a-dozen
others, accompanied by a profuse apology
from Sir Henry Turner, who explained that,
owing to the carelessness or stupidity of a
Creole clerk in the Governor's office, the letters
which arrived for Gerard a day or two after he
had left had been deposited in a pigeon-hole,
where they had only been accidentally discovered
some months later. " I take this
opportunity," Sir Henry went on, " of congratulating
you on your engagement, which I saw
in one of the papers. I could not help fancying,
when you came back from Venezuela, that
there was something more in the matter than
you cared to tell us just then ; and this is
a most interesting finale to your strange adventure.
I expect to be in England next
winter, and hope that we may then meet
again, and that you will present me to your
bride."

"But, George," continued Gerard, returning
to the question of his friend's marriage, " tell

US how it all came about. I felt sure all
along, you know, that there was some one in
your affair as well as in mine, and I'm so
awfully glad its all right now. Fancy our
each getting a wife from Netherdean, as it
were ! We didn't expect this that day we
first saw Florence with the harriers, did we ?
But how did you manage it, eh ? "

Ah, well, it's a long story," replied Morton,
with a smile — a peaceful, happy smile, so
different from the weary, cynical look of old
days — "but, in the end, I met her again by
the merest accident. I had about given, up
all hopes of seeing her again when I took to
the driving business, and it's curious that it
should have come about through that after
all. It was at the junction — Long Heaton,
you know, where I met you that time. I
was waiting for the south train one night,
just the same as when I saw you. It was
a pouring night, and I had got wet through
in my last run, and was feeling pretty wretched
and down on my luck. 1 was just loafing
about on the platform as the train came

in, and was looking at the people in the
carriages in a sort of stupid way, without
thinking of anything in particular, when all
of a sudden I caught sight of a face in a
carriage window that made my heart standstill.
Yes, there she was, sure enough ; you
could never mistake that face and that head
of hers. And she saw me, too ; and she
knew me, God bless her, all wet and grimy
and miserable as I was. I don't know what
happened exactly, but she spoke to me, and
she gave me her hand, and then the train
went off. I don't know how ever I got to
Starlington that night ; if it hadn't been for
old Zeke — my fireman, you know — there'd
have been some awful smash. I couldn't see
a signal, or anything, and we should have
come to the most awful grief; but the poor
old chap saw there was something wrong, and
he just did the whole business himself. As
soon as I got off duty, I went to the superintendent
and asked for leave, telling him that
something had happened, and that perhaps
I oughtn't come back again. He's a good

fellow, and squared it for me somehow, and I
went off by the next train on the other line.
She had told me where she was staying — near
Bedford — and I was there next morning.
Well, the long and the short of it is, Jerry, my
boy, I found she hadn't forgotten me all these
years, wretched devil that I was ; and if ever
there was an angel in this world, why, it's her.
You've had an easy time of it, old chap, compared
with me, and you can't quite understand
what it is to be brought to life again as it
were, after making up your mind that it's
about all up with you."

What a handsome couple they are, Jerry!"
said Florence to her lover, as they stood
together by the fire that evening after dinner,
Mrs Ashton being engaged in writing a note
in the next room. " Not quite so nice as us, of
course," looking archly at the reflection of her
own fair face in the glass. " But you know,
Jerry, she's really much handsomer than me,
and he — well, you're rather nice-looking, I
must say; but he's much more the sort of
man that women fall in love with than

you are, though perhaps you don't think
so!"

This sally, of course, provoked a little
lover-like demonstration from Gerard, after
which the two stood looking into the fire fora
few moments without speaking.

Jerry," resumed Florence, inserting her
arm through his, and clasping her hands in a
meditative manner, " do you know, I've got
an idea. I asked Mrs Caledon — Mrs Morton
I mean — where they were going to live, and
what they were going to do, and she said
they hadn't settled anything for certain at
present, but they had some thoughts of going
abroad for a time. Now, why shouldn't Mr
Morton go and live at Los Angelos ? You
said he had been in South America, and
knew all about cattle-farming and that sort
of thing ; and he could live there, and keep
up the place for us, and perhaps between us
we might be able to carry it on. I should
so like to keep it, if possible. I wonder if
she could live there ; of course, you know,
my mother " — in a somewhat hushed voice —

must have lived there, but then she was
a Spaniard, and, of course, used to a warm
climate. Oh, Jerry ! to think that I never
even saw her ! I do hope we shall find that
there is some picture of her somewhere. But
it might do, oughtn't it ? "

"My darling, it's a splendid idea," replied
Gerard, folding his arm round her waist,
and kissing the little clasped hands as they
rested on the mantelpiece. " Why, it would
be the very thing for old George. I don't
quite know about her, though ; still, ita
charming house, and I believe it isn't a
bad climate on the whole ; and if she didn't
mind being in such an out-of-the-way place
for a time, it might do. We'll think it
over, anyhow, and I'll sound George about
it."

Lord Ravendale's marriage came off early
in May, and was admitted on all sides to be
one of the great social events of the year.
Prominent among his attendant friends was
the "Cadger," gorgeously attired, and wearing
an expression eminently suited to the

occasion. He lead apparently got over his
supposed disappointment in regard to Lady
Adelaide, and his present to her was conspicuous
for its magnificence, even amidst
the brilliant display that pretty well filled
up the drawing - room in Eaton Square.
But it is generally believed that his feelings
have sustained an irreparable shock,
and that he will live and die a bachelor.

Good-bye, old fellow," said Lord Heavens
to his private secretary, as the latter
hovered about to receive his chiefs parting
instructions; "it will be your turn next.
Mind you keep him up to the mark. Lady
Florence, or hell perhaps take warning by
me, and make a bolt of it, eh ? However,
I'll be back soon, and then I'll keep an eye
on him for you."

It is hardly necessary to say that no one
had taken a warmer interest in Gerard's good
fortune than Lady Mount Easton.

"Well, Jerry," she said, when they met
for the first time after the announcement, " I
think you have really been most fortunate.

and I must say you have managed very well
for yourself. After all, the Civil Service hasn't
turned out so very badly for you, has it?
But you see I was quite right in advising you
to wait, for otherwise you might, perhaps,
have never gone to Venezuela, and you might
have spoiled the whole thing. So I think
everything has turned out for the best, and
you're a very lucky boy " — an opinion in
which Gerard was only too ready to concur.

When Florence's proposal was made known
to George Morton, his first thought was to
decline on his wife's account. But, somehow
or other, Constance herself was much taken
with the idea, and declared that it would
suit her admirably, that a warm climate had
always been recommended to her, and that
she could always come away for a change
when necessary. So after much discussion,
it was decided that the whole party should
go out to Los Angelos together in the
autumn, when Gerard could obtain sufficient
leave for the purpose, and that if, after
thorough inspection, it should appear to bea

suitable abode for Constance, the Mortons
should take charge of the property for a
year,* and then see whether any permanent
arrangement was likely to be a success.

Great was the excitement at the General
Enquiry Office when the result of Gerard's
expedition to San Fernando became known.
The lost charter, it should be observed, had
turned up in the most unexpected and mysterious
manner about a month after his
return. It was found one day lying on a
side table in the chief-clerk's room, attached
by a piece of red tape to a parliamentary
blue-book, containing "Further Papers relating
to the Maritime Jurisdiction of the Central
African Republic," but how it got there was
entirely beyond the power of any one in the
office to say. The last appointed junior clerk
was, it is true, so devoid of consideration and
knowledge of the world as to hint that it
might possibly have been there all the time.
This, however, was obviously a reflection, not
only upon the chief-clerk, but upon the whole
office, and no well-regulated mind could possibly

have entertained such an idea for a
moment. It is probable, therefore, that the
affair will always remain a mystery, as has
been the case with many other equally important
and significant occurrences in the
constitutional history of this country.

VOL. III.

It is a lovely evening in October — a loveliness
not of autumn, however, but as of some
mysterious blending together of spring and
summer, that is altogether unknown in northern
latitudes. For we are in the city of perpetual
spring, as Caracas, their exquisitely-situated
capital, is fondly termed by all true Venezuela.
A soft, golden light sheds its effulgence
over the airy spires and russet roofs
of the town, buried here and there in thick
groves of cactus and orange, and illumines
even the dark cypresses that hedge round a
quiet little burying ground on a hill-side,
where the tinkle of the bells from a flock of
goats up above, and the occasional shrill note
of a bird, are all the sounds that strike upon
the heavily-scented evening air.

Let us glance into a secluded nook, whereas

yet the ground has hardly been disturbed,
and where the luxuriantly flowering shrubs
straggle at will over the rocky ridges that
crop up here and there above the emerald
turf. It is not altogether untenanted, however,
this little corner of "God's Acre," for
here, girt round with a carefully - tended
border of tropical flowers, with a plain slab
of white marble at the head, and a little
cross of the same material at the foot, is
one solitary grave.

By it are standing two persons, their
hands clasped in each other's, and apparently
absorbed in silent contemplation. At last
one of them looks up in her companion's
face.

'* My darling," she whispers, " I should like
to be quite alone just for one minute ; you
won't mind, will you ? "

He presses her hand, and walks away to
the little stone wall that separates the
cemetery from the open hill-side. He looks
back for one moment, and sees a slender,
graceful figure kneeling by the marble headstone,
a gleam of sunset casting a golden

glory over her soft brown hair. Then
he leans over the wall, and gazes out upon
the fair prospect around him. The goats
advance in a semicircle, and stare at him
curiously, but he heeds them not ; and somehow,
his eyes seem dim, and he can only distinguish
a blurred outline of hill and grove,
with a soft blue mist hanging over the city
beneath. Suddenly a light footfall sounds behind
him, and, turning round, Florence is once
more by his side. There is a sweet smile on
her face, though her eyes are brimming over
with tears.

I am quite ready now, darling," she says,
softly, " but we'll just go back once more together."
And, taking his hand, she leads him
back to the grave.

As they turn to go away, a golden ray of
light strikes upon the headstone and some
characters that are carved upon it. Near the
top is one word only —

Isidore.

A little lower down is traced this inscription,
which is evidently of more recent
date —

FRANCIS, EIGHTH EARL OF Marlborough.

Born loth Sept. 1809.
Died igth Dec. 1879.
